MALAWI
SECOND EDUCATION SECTOR CREDIT
Credit and Project Summarv
Borrower:          Republic of Malawi
Beneficiaries:    Ministry of Education and Culture
Amount:            SDR 29.5 million (US$ 36.9 million)
Terms:             Standard, with 40 years maturity
Prolect Objectives:
The proposed Credit has four main objectives:
(a) to improve the quality of education at all levels;
(b) to expand access to primary and secondary education;
(c) to strengthen sector management, budgeting and planning; and
(d) to improve resource mobilization and allocation policies by
reversing the decline in the sector's budget share in accordance
with stated Government policy
Project Description:
The Credit would help the Government complete its funding
requirements for the Second phase of its Ten-Year Education
Investment Plan (1986-1995) which has been agreed with IDA. The
Credit will support the following: (1) quality improvement through
(a) curriculum reform and development, (b) improvements in the
preparation, production, and distrioution of textbooks and other
instructional materials, (c) development and introduction of new
methods for more effective use of radio in primary schools, (d)
improvement of the distance education system used at the secondary
level; (e) development and introduction of low cost approaches for
improving science instruction and the library system at the secondary
level; (f) improvement of teacher training; (g) expansion of the
number of qualified primary teachers, (h) reforms in the examination
system, and (i) strengthening the school inspection system; (2)
access improvements through (a) facilitating community self-help
upgrading of school facilities by providing roofing sheets antD
limited amounts of school furniture, and (b) planning and
implementing reforms in grade promotion criteria, and more intensive
utilization of facilities (double-shifts at secondary; and admission
of day students at the university) and (c) expansion of the
university's capacity to produce accountants - a skill in extremely
short supply; (3) strengthening management, budgeting, and planning
through (a) various institutional and capacity building measures in
the Ministry of Education and the Malawi Institute of Education, and
(b) a decentralization program for the Ministry; and (4) improved
resource mobilization and allocation through (a) reforms in fee



- {it-                                               I
structures at all levels, (b) changes in reporting requirements for
recurrent and investment expenditure plans, and (c) increares in the
share of the national budget allocated to Education.
Benefits and Risks:
The Credit will continue and further IDA support for policy re"orm in
education and will finance innovative programs to improve quality,
access, cost effectiveness, internal and external efficiency and
equity at all levels. Quality: the Credit will support the
introduction of more relevant curricula, more effective examination
systems and better learning materials in primary and secondary
schools. The frequency and quality of school supervision will
improve. Access and Equitys the Credit will promote greater access
at (a) the primary level directly benefitting rural children who have
dropped out of school because of pove=ty; and (b) secondary level
through introduction of double shift system and provision of
additional MCDE centers. Efficiency: in the medium and long term,
the Credit will improve internal efficiency by reducing repetition
and dropouts, thus lowering the cost per graduate. Management: the
planning, budgeting and management capabilities of the Ministry will
be strengthened.
The main risk is that Government would allocate insufficient
funds to the education sector over the next five years, leading to
delays in implementation of the reform program, the investment plan,
and the project. This risk has been minimized by requiring that a
commitment to adequate funding, supported uy Treasury and approval by
EP&D of budget projections, is in place before the project
implementation begins and that this be reaffirmed at regular
prescribed intervals during project implementation. A second risk is
that the limited managerial and administrative capability of the MOEC
to implement and effectively monitor the policy changes might be
overstretched by the project. The project is addressing this risk by
including measures to reinforce the planning function with more local
staff positions, support and technical assistance, and fellowships. A
third risk is that the large number of trainee teachers to be
recruited into the new three year in-service training program will
not all perform satisfactorily in the training program. This risk is
being addressed through a condition of credit effectiveness that
requires MOEC to develop criteria against which to evaluate their
academic and practical performance and to release from service those
who are unsatisfactory.

STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
SECOND EDUCATION SECTOR CREDIT
REPUBLIC OF MALAWI
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01     In 1985, the Government of Malawi (GOM), recognizing the crises it
faced in the education sector and in the macroeconomic situation, embarked on
a bold program of education reform and improvement, within the context of the
economic adjustment and recovery effort already underway. Through its
"Statement of Development Policies" (Devpol) for 1987-1996, together with the
Second Education Plan (1985/86-1994195) and associated ten year investment
program, and with IDA assistance under the First Education Sector Credit
(effective June 15, 1987), the GOM articulated and committed itself to a
comprehensive strategy for a decade of educational change, aware that it would
require a decade or more to complete.
1.02     The proposed Second Education Sector Credit would assist the GOM to
proceed with the second phase of this long-term plan, building upon and
extending the encouraging progress achieved in the first phase, while also
strengthening and intensifying the reform process where needed.
1.03     Through extensive consultations, reviews, and analysis undertaken to
guide its design and preparation, the project has taken into account that:
-  the economic environment and adjfistment program framework, while
still demanding strict austerity, is favorable for continuing the
education reform effort (Sec. A of Chapter I,
-  the Government objectives for the sector are sound, providing a
reasonable perspective as to where the sector should be heading in
the long run and an appropriate basis for setting priorities for
the short and medium run (Sec. B of Chapter I)
-  the main issues the GOM must address in the next ten years in
education pose difficult challenges requiring urgent and broad-
ranging measures responding to needs for adjustment,
revitalization, and eventual expansion (Chapter II)
-  the Government strategy and action plan for addressing these
issues through an updated ten-year investment and policy reform
program offer a sensible, sustainable and suitably comprehensive
framework for leading the sector into the 1990s (Chapter III)
-  the proposed credit, through supporting a time slice of the
investment plan and facilitating the next steps in the policy
reform process, would assist in fulfilling the program goals
(Chapter IV)



- the berefits of the project would be substantial; the risks, while
not insignificant, would be minimized by provisions included in
the design of the project (Chapter V)
- the agreements reached between the Government and IDA for this
proposed Credit encompass a wide range of issues, consistent with
the policy content and coverage of a time-slice of a ten-year
investment program (Chapter VI, and the Sector Policy Letter and
Matrix in Annex A).
The rest of this Introduction, focusing on the economic and policy
environment, and on Government strategy in education, outlines the context for
the more detailed look at the sector in subsequent chapters.
A. The Economic and Policy Environment
1.04     During the past decade, Malawi has had to contend with a series of
external shocks: falling world prices for exports; rapid escalation in import
prices, particularly fuel and intermediate and capital goods; an escalation
in regional political tensions which has disrupted external transport routes
and led to large rises in transport costs and caused a large and continuing
flow of Mozambican refugees to Malawi; poor weather and an infestation of the
cassava crop which have undermined food security. Severe foreign exchange
shortages have led to a build up of arrears on import payments, stringent
rationing of foreign exchange and import bottlenecks. The balance of payments
has come under severe strain, the Government budget deficit rose to
unsustainable levels and inflationary pressures have been strong.
1.05     The Government has responded with adjustment measures aimed at
restoring macro-economic stability and growth. These include: price
adjustments and decontrol; improved public sector resource management and
strict control of Government expenditures; exchange rate adjustments; export
promotion and diversification programs; strengthening of public sector policy
and planning capacity; and parastatal reform. Since 1981 these efforts have
been supported by three Structural Adjustment Loans, successive IMF stand-by
operations, an IMF extended facility and in 1988, a large Industrial and Trade
Policy Adjustment Credit and an IMF enhanced structural adjustment facility.
Despite periodic setbacks, Malawi has made good progress in implementing the
Government's adjustment program. Real GDP contracted by 6 percent in 1980-
1981, recovered to grow at an annual average rate of 3.1 percent through 1986,
but deciined by about 1.5 percent in 1987. Growth in 1988 at 3.6 percent
exceeded expectations, and growth is projected to accelerate to between 4 and
5 percent in the short-term making possible a gradual recovery in real per
capita incomes.
1.06     Macroeconomic adjustment exerts its most direct impact on the
education sector through Government budgetary allocations. The large and
increasing external debt burden has been crowding out public expenditure and
restraining the growth in budgetary allocations for new investments and
iecurrent expenditures. Interest payments have claimed an average of more



-3 -
than 17 percent of total Government expenditures each year from 1982 to
1988. 1/ Interest payments are projected to remain at their 1988/89 level
(in Kwacha terms) through 1991/92, implying that they will claim a deezeasing
share of total expenditure, leaving room for some recovery in sectoral
expenditure shares.
1.07     The education sector has a major role to play in achieving the three
key economic and social objectives outlined in Devpol: (i) the reduction of
poverty, ignorance and disea4e by achieving rapid and sustained economic
growth; (ii) an improvement in income distribution; and (iii) stabilization of
economic growth. Devpol explicitly states that "education is seen as the most
fundamental long-term investment in economic progress and social as well as
political cohesion". Government's ability to achieve the quality improvements
and expansion in access to education required to fulfill its objectives will
be greatly affected by the availability of external financing and of domestic
resources.
B. Government Strate&v in Education
1.08     Since independence in 1964, the development of education has been an
integral part of a wider national policy. The broad policy objective for the
sector as contained ia Devpol is to develop an efficient and high quality
system of education of a type and size appropriate to available resources and
to the political, social and economic aspirations of the nation. Three
central concerns are :  (a) ensuring access to educational opportunities; (b)
proper balance in the allocation of resources to the different subsectors of
the system; and (c) improving the quality and efficiency of the system.
1.09     During the First Education Plan period (originally 1973-1980 and then
extended to 1984), there was considerable expansion at all levels. The
primary level gross enrollment target of 50 percent was substantially
exceeded. This was a consequence of greater demand for primary education
(and, hence enrollment) than anticipated, and was not matched by increases in
inputs such as trained teachers, instructional materials, textbooks and
physical facilities. As a result, there was considerable deterioration in
quality. The secondary gross enrollment rate rose from 3 to 5 percent - much
below the original target of 15 percent, but commensurate with the manpower
needs of the modern economy.
1.10     Government has explicitly considered the manpower needs of the
economy in deciding on the pace and pattern of post-primary expansion. The
high rates of unemployment and underemployment of secondary school and
University graduates typical of many other African countries have been
avoided, although this problem is beginning to emerge. Taking account of
present economic conditions and prospects, particularly the expected slow
growth in wage employment, the Government sees a need to shift the emphasis
towards preparing students for self-employment and entrepreneurship rather
than for conventional employment.
1/ All data are taken from the Malawi Public Expenditure Review, IBRD, Report
No. 7281-MAI, 1989.



1.11     The Government's Second Educational Plan (1985/86 - 1994/95) is more
comprehensive and detailed than the first. 2/ Progress has been made during
the first three years of the Second Education Plan in initiating curricula
reform at the primary level, in provision of textbooks and teach<'s guides,
and in expanding access and in teacher education. Total enrollment at the
primary level increased from 899,459 in 1984185 to 1,022,765 in 1986/87 (a
growth rate of 4.4 percent per annum). Secondary school enrollment increased
from 24,343 in 1984/85 to 26,183 in 1986/87 (2.5 percent per year).
Enrollment at the university has increased slightly from 1964 to 2177 (3.5
percent per year), and technical/vocational college enrollment increased from
500 to 717 (15.8 percent per year). Primary teacher training enrollment fell
from 1,920 to 1,802 as a result of unusually high dropouts, no-shows and late
selection.
t/ See World Bank Staff Appraisal Report No. 6428-MAI (February 24, 1987)
Education Sector Credit 1767, pp.13-17, for a summary of the Second Ten-Year
Education Plan.

II.  THE EDUCATION AND TRAINING SEfTOR

issuess

2.02      The information, observations, and conclusions presented in this
chapter are based on (a) review and analysis of the extensive assessments and
other literature that exist now on education in Malawi, (b) new data
collection and evaluation undertaken for this project, and (c) thorough
consultations between Government and IDA staff. The literature includes staff
appraisal reports, project completion reports and other documentation of the
preceding six IDA investments in the sector, and sector reviews (see Annex J).
Working from this knowledge base, the project preparation effort arrived at
the list of issues discussed here after first reviewing a larger list of
potential problem areas, covering every aspect of education from primary
through post-second_,.ry and, in somewhat less detail, the formal and informal
training system. The issues discussed in Sections B through F below, after
the background description of the Section A, are recognized by the Government
as the principal areas where it needs to concentrate its efforts in the next
ten years.
A. Basic Characteristics of the Sector
2.03      The Ministry of Education and Culture (MOEC) is responsible for all
formal education and training in Malawi. This includes primary, secondary,
technical and post-secondary institutions such as the Primary Teacher Training
Colleges and the Malawi College of Accountancy (MCA). It is also responsible
for the non-formal system of secondary education (Malawi College of Distance
Education, MCDE). In addition, MOEC has formal responsibility for the
University of Malawi, Malawi Institute of Education (MIE), and the Malawi
National Examination Board (MANEB), although in practice this responsibility
is exercised through its membership in the Management Boards of such bodies.
2.04      The education system is divided into four major levels:  primary
(standards 1-8) with a final Primary School Leaving Certificate (PSLC)
examination at the end of the eight years; junior secondary (Forms I-II)
leading to a Junior Certificate (JC); senior secondary (Forms III-IV) at the
end of which the students sit the Malawi Certificate of Education examination



- 7 -
(MCE); and tertiary including 4-5 years of university education and two years
of teacher training (Chart 1). At the primary level, local communities are
largely responsible for the physical facilities, while at the secondary level,
church-affiliated NGOs play an important role. Schooling is not compulsory
nor is there an age attendance limit. Private schooling accounts for about 6
percent of primary school enrollment and 16 percent of secondary school
enrollment. In 1987 MCDE enrolled about 17,000 students in its 105 study
centers, about 2000 in its 17 night secondary schools and a further 5000
studying at home. The Government has a functional literacy program supported
by LriICEF aimed at making 2 million adults literate by 1995.
2.05      The MOEC is divided into four administrative units under the general
supervision of the Principal Secretary assisted by a Deputy Secretary. These
are: Planning, the Education Inspectorate, Education Administration, and the
Accounts and Personnel Department (Chart 2). The Inspectorate is responsible
for secondary school inspection while Education Administration is responsible
for inspecting and administering primary schools, primary teacher training,
technical schools and distance education. Partial decentralization has
resulted in the creation of three "Regional Education Offices (REO) and 28
District Education Offices (DEO). Field supervision of primary schools is
carried out by regional and district level staff using guidelines provided by
headquarters. Secondary school administration and field level supervision,
primary teacher training, technical/vocational training and distance education
are all centralized at headquarters. The responsibility of the planning
department includes identification, preparation, supervision and evaluation of
education projects, policy studies and preparation of education statistics.
2.06      The MOEC also provides technical and vocational education through
the technical colleges and Polytechnic Board of Governors courses. These
courses are administered jointly with the Ministry of Labor which is primarily
responsible for meeting private sector formal training needs. At present all
major ministries have training units. Most operating ministries provide
vocational training for their own skilled workers -- notably, agricultural and
veterinarian assistants in the Ministry of Agriculture and health and nursing
assistants in the Ministry of Health. Public administration courses are
provided by institutions under the Department of Personnel Management and
Training (DPMT).
2.07      Enrollment in the five secondary and post-secondary vocational and
technical education institutions increased from 500 to 777 between 1984/85 and
1986/87. Enrollment levels are determined by the Ministry of Labour on the
basis of perceived market demand, and have been limited to far below capacity
because of very slow industrial growth. Even so, some trainees have
difficulty finding jobs after graduation. The planned transfer of the Board
of Governors technician training program from the Polytechnic to the technical
schools should improve utilization levels. Trainees acquire some practical
experience during the in-plant segment of the program. However, inadequate
supervision in some work places makes it important for colleges to provide
hands-on experience as well. Inadequate supplies of materials prevents this.
2.08      The education system has made enormous progress since independence
in educating and training Malawians to fill posts in the civil service and to
provide the skilled and educated labor force needed by the growing modern
economy. Replacement of expatriates has been considerable, and most of those



- 8 -
who remain have skills and expertise that would be expensive or difficult to
provide in-country. The present capacity of the training system is adequate
for the present and projected future demand for educated and skilled manpower,
except in some specialized fields where shortages persist -- notably
accountants and physicians.
B. Financing Issues
2.09      The Need to Increase Public Expenditure on Education.  Government
spending on education is exceptionally low, even in comparison with other
African countries.  Total Government expenditure on education is around 3
percent of national income in Malawi, compared to over 5 percent in Kenya and
Zambia, nearly 7 percent in Zimbabwe, and more than 8 percent in Botswana.
Real per capita spending on education has fallen steadily from K14 in 1983/84
to K10 in 1988/89 (in 1987 constant prices). Rapid population growth (3.5
percent between 1977 and 1987) has created enormous pressure on educational
facilities, and considerable additional resources will be necessary to
maintain preseat (low) enrollment levels, let alone to satisfy some of the
unmet demand for education. The school age population has been growing at 4.8
percent each year during the past decade, and will continue to grow at least
at this rate for the neat decade.

2.10      The education share of Government expenditure is below 12 percent,
much less than most sub-Saharan countries (the median African country
allocated 18.5 percent of its budget to education in 1980, and 15.3 percent in
1983). 2/ Table 2.1 shows that the education share of total Government
expenditure fell steadily from 12.9 percent in 1983 to 10.1 percent in 1986,
and recovered only some of the loss to reach 10.4 percent in 1987 and 11.1
percent in 1988. Planned investments in the sector have been repeatedly
delayed and recurrent expenditures have been chronically and seriously
underfunded. The underfunding of education recurrent expenditures has
worsened in real terms: despite expansion of the sector, the recurrent budget
allocation to education rose by an average of less than 1 percent per year
between 1982/83 and 1985/86, and the substantial rise in 1986/87 was reversed
in subsequent years.
2.11      The Government development policy historically has given the
directly productive sectors priority over the social sectors. During the
difficult fiscal time since 1982, education has fared worse than other sectors
in terms of real expenditure. Table 2.2 shows the annual change in real
Government expenditure allocated to education compared with other selected
sectors. Between 1982 and 1986, the education allocation grew less, or fell
more in real terms than that of the other social sectors (health and community
services). The same is true comparing education with the other (economic)
sectors and agriculture in particular, except in certain of the earlier years.
In 1987/88 the education real budgetary allocation fell slightly less than
that of the other sectors, barring agriculture.

2.12      However, the policy framework for the coming decade as set out in
Devpol affirms the Government commitment to giving more priority to education,
and gradually increasing education budget share to 15 percent. To reverse the
decline of the recent past and increase education expenditure to at least a
minimally adequate level to meet the basic needs of a rapidly growing
population is of vital importance to (i) Malawi's economic development
generally; (ii) to consolidating the gains of the past in quality and
enrollments; and (iii) financing other essential improvement measures.
Furthermore, the other improvement measures that need to be undertaken will
come to little, disabled by lack of resources.
2.13      There is strong economic and social justification for increasing the
resources devoted to education. Estimated social rates of return to
educational outlays (using 1981 data) are 14 percent for primary, 15-20
percent for secondary, and 11 percent for the University (Annex Table 1).
Other things being equal, more educated women tend to have better nourished
and better educated children and lower fertility levels. This effect is
compounded by the positive relationship between education and earnings. A
large body of research in developing and developed countries consistently
shows a strong relationship between education and worker productivity. The
more educated farmers in Malawi have been found to have higher maize yields
(in large part because they have much more contact with the extansion service)
and to grow a greater diversity of crops, suggesting that education has a
positive impact on agricultural development. 3/  A more educated, literate
population is easier and cheaper to reach through mass media and other public
information campaigns, tend to adopt improved practices in health and
agriculture (for example) more readily and are better able to learn new skills
and to take advantage of new opportunities for generating income.
2.14      Resource Allocation Within the Education Budtet: Priorities for the
1990s. Even if the educatioai share of total Government expenditures rises as
envisaged in Devpol, resource availability will remain very highly
constrained. This increases the importance of careful allocative decisions,
both across and within different levels of the education system. The
allocation of the recurrent budget to different levels reflects the increasing
priority being placed on primary education, whose 1988189 share (47.5 percent)
is higher than ever before. The University share has been reduced from nearly
one-quarter of the budget, to 18.5 percent. The allocation to the secondary
level has fallen from around 17 percent to between 15 and 16 percent over the
past ten years, and within the secondary level there has been a small (but
inadequate) growth in allocations to MCDE.
2.15      Budgetary allocations should take account of the relative cost-
effectiveness in terms of educational outcome of various expenditures. For
example, evidence from Africa and other developing countries suggests that the
impact on learning per dollar spent on textbooks is higher than on most other
inputs. There may be strong interdependencies among expenditure categories.
For example, large outlays on teacher training and wages may fail to have the
expected impact on educational quality if there are inadequate teaching
3/ Perraton, Jamison, . akins, Orivel and Wolff, 1983, Basic Education and
Agricultural Extension, Costs. Effects and Alternatives, IBRD Staff Working
Paper No. 564, ppl87-195.



- 11 -
materials and textbooks. Realistic and careful planning and forward budgeting
could prevent such non-wage items being squeezed out between rising wage bills
and constrained budgetary growth.
2.16      Consistency between Planned Investment and the Recurrent Budget.
The Government budgetary restraint has had a greater impact on the development
budget than on the recurrent budget (see Table 2.3). In real terms, the
recurrent budget increased by a very small 3 percent overall from 1,82/83 to
1984185, rose 13 percent in 1986J87 and then fell back by 1988189 to the
1982/83 - 1985/86 real level. The real value of the development budget fell a
massive 24 percent between 1982/83 and 1984/85, increased by a small 2 percent
in the following year, and fell by 27 and 18 percent in the subsequent two
years. The allocation for 1988/89 restores the real level by 18 percent, but
this is little more than half the real level in 1982/83. These trends have
resulted in a more sustainable balance between recurrent and development
expenditures. However, this adjustment was less deliberate than it was a
reflection of the much greater ease of postponing investments than of cutting
recurrent expenditures.

2.17      Investments in additional capacity which are not sustained by
adequate levels of recurrent financing do not generate their full potential
benefits, and may therefore show a low return. At present, the development
budget tends to emerge as the sum of investments which various donors have
agreed to finance, without MOEC first deciding on an investment plan in
accordance with clearly defined development priorities. It is vital that the
Ministry formulate medium- and long-term investment plans and rolling five-
year annual development and expenditure budgets. The planning and budgeting
process must explicitly consider the recurrent cost implications of
investments and include these as a criterion in choosing among proposed
investments. This should result in a development plan that reflects the
Ministry's educational priorities and is sustainable given resource
limitations.
2.18      Cost Recovery throuzh Fees: The Next Phase.  Fees and other income
reflected in the 1988/89 education budget covered only 1.7 percent of
recurrent expenditure   When primary school fees and University fees (which
are not shown in the budget) are added in, cost recovery rises to 8.6 percent.
Parents also bear non-fee costs such as uniforms, pencils and exercise books.
An estimated MK 4.08 million was collected by primary schools in 1988/89.
Although modest in relation to the total recurrent budget, this added nearly
11 percent to the primary level allocation.  Secondary school tuition fees
have remained at only 30 kwacha per year for some time, less than 8 percent of
the 1988/89 unit cost of day schools, and far below fees charged by private
schools. Secondary school boarding charges (recently increased to 14K 150 per
year) cover only 30 percent of the unit cost differential between boarding and
day schools. Fees at MCDE cover 27 percent of total recurrent costs. Except
for students (mainly females) pursuing the commercial and secretarial courses,
technical/vocational college students pay no fees at all.  University students
make no contribution to the costs of their board and lodging, and the net
tuition fee of MK200 per student instituted in 1989 represents only 3 percent
of unit costs.
2.19      Primary fees were last raised in 1982183.  Inability to pay primary
school fees is believed to keep many children from enrolling at school, to
contribute to the high dropout rate, and to cause interruptions in children's
schooling which often result in their repeating standards. The Government
intends to reduce or abolish fees at the lower primary standards. This
decision is consistent with the recommendations of the World Bank Policy Study
Education in Sub-Saharan Africa. The impact on enrollment, dropout and
repetition needs to be carefully monitored. On the other hand, there is
considerable unmet demand for post-primary places, even within the high cost
private secondary education subsector. MCDE students and their parents have
shown their willingness to pay for lower quality secondary education than is
provided in formal secondary schools. There are reportedly seven applicants
for each available place in the University. Unmet demand and high private
benefit/cost ratios for secondary and post-secondary educatior. suggest
considerable scope for increased student or parental contributions at these
levels. A first step has been taken by stopping University student allowances
and instituting a MK200 net tuition payment, and introducing a loan scheme for
indigent students. However, cost recovery is still inadequate.



- 13 -
2.20      Resource Mobilization through Community Self-Help and Other Non-
Governmental Sources: Opportunities and Challenges. In the past, Government
has left the responsibility for primary school construction, furnishing and
equipping to local communities. However, many schools have remainod
incomplete because communities could not afford imported materials such as
roofing sheets. Government has recently begun assisting community self-help
efforts by providing imported materials paid for with bilateral aid (USAID,
Canada) and multilateral financing (AfDE and financial IDA Third Education
Project (Credit 910-MAI) and the First Education Sector Credit (Credit 1767-
MAI)). Community response has been much better than anticipated, and
available funding is insufficient to meet the requests for assistance.
Government would like to meet the communities' requests for imported materials
to finish primary classrooms already begun. Since Habitat for Humanity has
now developed suitable cheap roofing tiles in the country, Government should
encourage communities to use this cheap roofing material instead of imported
corrugated iron sheets (Annex G).
2.21      Malawi has a tradition of mission or church supported secondary
schools. These schools enjoy grant-aided status. Government can do more to
encourage churches and communities to build secondary schools. At present,
community participation has not been fully exploited and is limited to
provision of boarding facilities.  Given the high demand for secondary school
places, Government can, as in other African countries, encourage self-help
construction of secondary schools by offering to supply teachers and equipment
to communities with completed classrooms and teachers' houses.
2.22      Financial Manatement and Planning: Inadequate Capabilities and
Procedures. There are only about 25 registered qualified Malawian
accountants; the large number of expatriates in this profession do not fully
meet the shortage. The public sector is particularly affected as it is unable
to compete against the high salaries and large fringe benefits offered in the
private sector. This results in understaffed accounting and financial control
departments and staff without the training necessary to ensure high standards
of financiai management and control. The MOEC has coped well, and has good
financial information systems. However, the management culture of the
Ministry and the staffing problem have resulted in excessive centralisation.
Routine and trivial matters overload the small Ministry headquarters
accounting office, creating unnecessary paperwork and delays, and leaving less
time for financial planning and management activities (see also para 2.44).
C.  Qualitv Issues
2.23      Curriculum Reform: Vitally Needed and a Difficult. Long-Term
Undertaking.  The curricula at the primary school level are overly
theoreti4al, emphasize memorization at the expense of understanding concepts,
and depend only on textbooks instead of using a range of teaching and learning
aids.  The curricula are also heavily overloaded and needlessly
compartmentalized into an excessive number of subjects.  Ten or more of the
timetabled subjects receive perfunctory treatment in the one hour allotted to
them per week.  Because of the overlap system, the lower primary classes
receive as little as 15 hours of instruction a week. Curriculum revision has
been confined almost exclusively to production of teachers guides based on
existing texts. A detailed examination of the content of approved texts and



- 14 -
their adaptation to the realities of Malawi is long overdue. MOEC has now
begun a careful review and revision of the curriculum. A compilation of
objectives, and scope and sequence chart for each subject area have been
completed. By September 1989, the teaching syllabi for each subject area for
all standards will be completed. At the secondary school level, there are
also too many subjects. At both the JC and MCE levels, students are required
to take eight prescribed core subjects and up to three out of seven optional
subjects (Annex Table 2). In practice, the number of subjects offered in any
school is determined by staff and physical resources. The number of subjects
needs to be reduced. Curricula require updating especially in mathematics,
science and agriculture.
2.24      Instructional Materials (Textbooks, etc.): Too Few and Outdated.
Textbooks and exercise books are in chronically short supply at all levels.
Pupil/book ratios, especially at the primary level are worse than in
neighboring countries (4:1 compared to 2:1 in Botswana). Many primary school
children receive only half an exercise book a year. Some textbooks were
revised and availability increased with IDA assistance. However, the
substantial increase in school enrollment and lack of budgetary resources for
replacement has again left many students without textbooks.  Government has
agreed to an earmarked budgetary allocation of K2 per prima-y school child for
textbooks. The parental contribution of Kl per pupil annually for textbooks
replacement and their supplementary fee contributions for stationery are
totally inadequate for these needs. Stationery is severely limited or
unavailable especially in the early years of primary education. At the
secondary level, textbook shortages are compounded by lack of equipment and
supplies, particularly for science courses. Libraries are not well stocked
and student use of the libraries is not integrated into the curriculum.
2.25      Textbooks are all published by Dzuka, a small publishing subsidiary
of Blantyre Printing and Packaging, which has a monopoly of local textbook
printing. During the last ten years, Dzuka has originated less thaz, one title
a year and their main activity has been controlling the very substantial
reprint program of MOEC. MIE publishing activity is limited by their printing
capacity, which can cope with a run of only 3,000. Printing and publishing
involving longer print runs are passed to Dzuka. Arrangements have been ad
hoc, with the production manager typically receiving instructions from
individual subject specialists. Equally there has been no pricing policy and
little negotiation with outside suppliers. It is essential to institute a
workable publishing system at MIE. Textbook distribution is carried out by
Malawi Book Service (MBS), a parastatal body established in 1964 to service,
assist and promote the education system by acting as central suppliers of
educational supplies to schools and other educational establishments in
Malawi, as well as to retail traders. At present, their textbook distribution
service consists mainly in delivering reprints from Blantyre Print and
Packaging to the three Regional Education offices (REOs). For this service,
MBS has obtained a discount of 20 percent on a net price charged by Dzuka.
MOEC orders the books via MBS, and pays the full net price to MBS on
production of the endorsed delivery note.
2.26      The current situation whereby MBS delivers to the three REOs is
inadequate and unsatisfactory. First, the REOs do not have adequate storage
facilities, and deliveries cause space problems in offices and corridors,
whereas MBS has its own warehouse in each of the three regional centers, which



- 15 -
would constitute a far more suitable base for regional distribution. Second,
the REOs lack distribution expertise and resources, and books typically
languish in these offices for a month while waiting for transport. Thiro, MBS
already makes deliveries of consumables directly to the District Education
Offices and is willfng and able to deliver the textbooks as well. This
service would save time and reduce costs.
2.27      Teachers: Deficiencies in their Numbers and Training.  Teacher
availability at the primary level has not kept pace with enrollment expansion.
In 1986187 the pupil:teacher ratio in primary schools was 63:1 compared with
60:1 in 1984/85. The uneven distribution of teazhers causes this ratio to
vary widely from 48:1 in Mzuzu city to 128:1 in Mchinji and 119:1 in Dowa.
Urban areas are at a decided advantage.  To attain the target teacher:pupil
ratio of 55:1 at the current enrollment level would require an additional
20,500 teachers.  The ratio of pupils to qualified teachers improved from 87:1
to 76:1 between 1984/85 and 1986/87, but an estimated 17 percent of primary
teachers are still untrained. The Government estimates that permanent houses
are only available to about 8,000 of the 13,405 qualified teachers in the
country. Most rural schools lack decent housing and therefore have difficulty
in attracting qualified teachers. It is Government policy to require
communities to construct teachers' houses through self-help but this is not a
priority for many communities. Government needs to devise an incentive system
to get communities to provide houses, so as to be able to attract competent
and well qualified teachers to disadvantaged rural areas. The quality of
teacher training needs improving in some areas (para 3.25).
2.28      The training of primary school teachers has deficiencies in numerous
areas: (a) relevance of the curriculum especially in regard to (i) the primary
education syllabus, (ii) adequate balance between professional/ pedagogical
F         and academic/subject content, and (iii) preparation to teach standard one; (b)
training sufficient numbers of teachers in a cost effective way; (c) adequacy
of the design of the ongoing one year teacher upgrading program and parity of
qualifications with the two year program; (e) systematic evaluation of teacher
education programs; and (f) internal efficiency. Despite achievement in these
areas, some issues remain unresolved. Curriculum: it would be advisable to
raise the academic standard required of entrants, rather than trying to make
up for student academic deficiencies by increasing the subject content of
training. The curriculum neglects aspects of professional training such as
met-hods for teaching very large classes, remedial and multigrade teaching.
Teaching realities and new policies will increasingly require an understanding
of these concepts. Supply: The estimated future annual trained teacher
output of 1,700 is not sufficient to meet present and projected demand.
Demand will be substantially increased by growing primary school enrollment
and the Government's intention to phase out untrained primary school staff and
ito reduce the pupil:teacher ratio from 67:1 to 55:1. It is estimated that by
1990 there will be a gap of over 4,000 trained teachers. In order to close
the gap MOEC has two options: (a) establish new colleges at high capital and
recurrent costs and with the risk of excess capacity in future; or (b) provide
less expensive in-service training which, given adequate inputs, can achieve
results comparable with pre-service training. One year in-service
training/upzrading program: UNICEF has recently provided funds for upgrading a
small number of untrained teachers in this program. MOEC has recently
evaluated and revised the program stru'sure. The pcor examination performance
might well be because the one-year program emphasized teaching methodology



- 16 -
rather than academic content and because trainees in the upgrading program
tend to be academically weaker than their counterparts in the regular program.
Internal efficiency: The trainee selection procedures need improvement to
ensure thas all colleges are filled within two weeks of the commencement of
the academic year, thus improving internal efficiency.
2.29      At the secondary level, the issues in teacher training are quite
different. The secondary student teacher ratio is 21:1, but there is a
shortage of science and mathematics teachers. Many teachers are required to
teach subjects for which they are unqualified. The required teaching
qualification is a university degree or diploma. The degree program
essentially prepares teachers to teach only one subject which restricts their
use outside their specialty. As a result, many teachers are underutilised,
with an average weekly teaching load of 25-26 periods out of the 45 timetabled
periods (para 3.29). The university has recently restructured the training
program to improve quality and began to implement an in-service mathematics
and science teacher upgrading program in September 1988. Producing enough
science and math teachers is constrained by lack of suitable candidates from
the secondary school system.
2.30      Examinations:  Movina in the Ritht Direction But Slowly.  School
examinations in Malavs  serve two functionst  (a) tsney certify that students
have successfully completed schooling at the various levels; and (b) they
select students for higher levels of the education system. Three shortcomings
of the examinations are: (a) they tend to reflect the rote learning approach
evident in the school system, (b) there is inadequate feedback to schools on
student performance; and (c) delays In examination results cause late
selection of students for higher levels of education. Preparation,
administration and marking of examinations are undertaken by the Malawi
National Examination Board (MANEB). MANEB is constrained by a lack of
printing facilities and staff. It has to make ad hoc use of teachers and
inspectors, and has had to preempt the printing facilities of MCDE for 6
months of the year (preventing MCDE from meeting its own printing
requirements).   Multiple choice questions could be used for a significant
proportion of the examinations to increase reliability, reduce costs, and
shorten marking time. It is recognised that developing multiple choice test
questions is not easy and needs to be undertaken with great care.
2.31      Selection to Formi I is based on the PSLC examination, which may not
ensure that the best students are selected. The examination emphasizes rote
learning, fails to test higher cognitive skills, and gives an advantage to
standard eight repeaters who have two or more years to memorize enough
information to gain high scores. Although the quality of secondary education
is generally high, in 1987 only 30 percent of Form IV students taking the MSCE
scored high enough marks to qualify for entrance to the University.
Performance was particularly poor in mathematics and science, where (1986)
pass rates were only 9.6 percent and 16.9 percent respectively.
2.32      School Supervision/Inspection and Manaaementt A Lon  Wary To Go.
Primary school teachers do not get adequate supervision and guidance. There
are only 55 inspectors - one per 300 teachers - and 47 home economics
organizers for a system with over 16,000 teachers. This is far short of the
Ministry's goal of one inspector per 150-200 teachers. There is a general
need to improve the understanding among inspectors and school headmasters of



- 17 -
how to supervise and advise teachers. The inspectorate lacks (a) funds for
subsistence payments, and (b) transport, and is preoccupied with other duties,
such as examinations and curriculum development, which limits field time to
only six months during the year, and does not allow regular visits to all
schools. It is not unusual for a teacher to be without a single class visit
by an inspector for two years. A curriculum is only as good as the teachers
who implement it and any curriculum reform process must include an in-service
teacher training component. The extent of training should be determined by
the qualification of teachers and the extent to which they are expected to
modify their teaching practices. Regular school supervision and school-based
teacher training are critical to the success of the curriculum reform process.
Although MIE has an upgrading program for headmasters, this training is
limited. Some training in school management and planning is offered to
headmasters, but this is totally inadequate. With the establishment of the
Malawi Institute of Management (MIM), MOEC hopes to organize systematic
training for headmasters on school planning and management.
2.33      Distance Education: Quality is Improving but More Can and Should be
Done. Distance education is well-established in Malawi, through the Malawi
College of Distance Education. However, the quality of education offered at
the MCDE is in need of substantial improvement. MCDE uses a three way
teaching system: it combines printed and audio self-instructional materials
with some face to face teaching. The printed materials are the main aid to
learning. Unfortunately, however, many of the students do not receive all the
correspondence course materials and many cannot listen to the radio programs
because the radios are not functioning. Furthermore, the quality of the
courses and the programs are variable. Students working at home receive very
little help while those studying in the night secondary schools have only two
hours of teaching per day and even this is of doubtful quality. Students in
study centers are taught in overcrowded classrooms by teachers without subject
expertise. MCDE, under-resourced and regarded by many as inferior education,
tries to reach too many target groups through too many modes of instruction.
With ODA assistance, MCDE has recently developed a three-year plan for
management and quality improvement. MCDE should concentrate its efforts and
resources where it is most successful -- that is, JC courses offered at study
centers, which are an effective alternati e to formal secondary schools. The
night secondary schools are not functioning well and students at home have
very low examination pass rates.
2.34      The Learning Environment:  School Buildings. Furniture and Equipment
are Grossly Inadequate. Adequate facilities are required if the quality of
education is to be improved. There is only one classroom per 70 students on
average and the situation is worse in urban areas where the norm is 100 pupils
per classroom. To achieve the desired ratio of 1 classroom per 50 pupils at
the present enrollment level would require 5,900 new permanent classrooms to
meet the present shortfall, and 1,000 - 1300 additional new classrooms each
year to accommodate a 5 percent increase in enrollment - a total of nearly
13,000 new classrooms during the remaining 6 years of the Second Educational
Plan (see para 3.28). Primary schools lack furniture - there is only one
chair per 15 pupils and one desk per 12 pupils - and are deficient in learning
and teaching materials. These conditions militate against effective learning.



- 18 -
D.   Access and Eouitv Issues
2.35      Enrollment Levels:  Low and Prevented from Expanding Faster -- Due
to the Severe Constraints in Resources (Classrooms. Teachers. etc.).
Enrollments are still very low, even in comyparison with other countries in the
region. At the primary level, the gross enrollment ratio was 55 percent in
1986187, and net enrollment was 43 percent. The Government hopes to raise the
net enrollment ratio to 85 percent by 1995. For many, the constraint on
access at the primary level is lack of a schcol nearby; for some, it is the
cost of school attendance. Behtind that problem lies another -- the resource
constraints that limit the numbers of schools that can be built and
maintained, and the number of teachers that can be afforded.
2.36      At the secondary level, Malawi like other countries in the region,
has kept the number of places small, while historically it gave more emphasis
to building up primary enrollment. Gross enrollment for the secondary level
age group is about 4.5 percent. The only facilities are: (a) 32 Government or
Government-aided day schools (most of which, with or without Government help,
have developed board.   facilities); (b) 27 Government or Government-aided
boarding schools; (c) .o unassisted schools; and (d) about 105 distance
education centers and night secondary schools. There are insufficient
secondary school places for qualified students: only 11 percent of the 66,000
students who passed the PSLC Examination in 1987 obtained places in secondary
Form I, and a further 14 percent enrolled in MCDE centers. The selection
procedure for the upper secondary cycle (Forms 3-4) puts iCDE pupils at a
disadvantage. Formal secondary school students who pass JC are given
priority, and only the few residual places are open to MCDE students.
2.37      Female Participation: Improving But More Progress Needed.
Enrollments of girls have improved considerably at primary and secondary
levels in the past decade, as shown in the table below:
Table 2.4
FEMALE PARTICIPANTS AS A PERCENTAGE OF ENROLLMENT
1980      1984      1987
Primary                      30        42         44
Secondary                    20        29         34
University                   20        21         23
Primary Teacher Training    37         37         35
Source:  Government of Malawi:  Education Statistics, 1980, 1984, 1987.
However dropout rates for girls are considerably higher than for boys, and
there remains room for improvement in enrollment at post-primary levels. The
most acute shortfall in enrollment is among girls of primary school age in
rural areas, where girls account for only 20 percent of total enrollment. The
long history of low female enrollment is reflected in much higher illiteracy
rates among women than men - 83 percent compared to 56 percent according to
1977 census data for persons of 15 years and older. Improvements during the
past decades in the participation of women in primary education are reflected
in slightly lower illiteracy in the age group of 15 to 24 years.



- 19 -
2.48      Distributional Disparitiess Some Groups And Districts Still Better
Serviced Than Others. Access to education is unevenly distributed among the
regions.  Two-thirds of primary school age children in the northern and
central regions are enrolled, compared to only one third in the southern
region.  Within regions, there are marked differences between urban and rural
areas. Net enrollment in urban Lilongwe is 138 percent but only 39 percent in
rural Lilongwe. Net enrollment is 98 percent in urban Blantyre compared with
43 percent in the surrounding rural area.
E.  Efficiency Issues
2.39      Primary Repetition and Dropout Rates are Too High. The Internal
efficiency of the education system is low, particularly at the primary level.
Only 23 percent of the children who enter standard one complete eight years
and it takes an average of sixteen years to produce a standard eight graduate,
which doubles the cost of producing a primary school graduate. Dropout
averages 13 percent for all grades during the year and is especially high in
standard one. Overall, the retention rate between standard one and three is
only 48 percent. The 1987/88 repetition rate was 20 percent; it is also
particularly high in standard one (24 percent) and in standard eight (48
percent). Higher zepetition rates in standard one are associated with
excessively large classes, lack of teaching materials and teachers who are not
trained to deal with very large classes. An estimated 10-15 percent of
children dropout after the first term when fees are due, which adds to the
repetition rate when some of these children reenter the system. Students
repeat the eighth grade (sometimes 7-8 times) trying to improve their PSLC
scores enough to gain entry to secondary school. It is essential that MOEC
implement policies that will reduce repetition and dropout rates (para 3.33).
2.40      Pupil/Teacher Ratios: Too High in Primary and Too Low in Secondary
and Post-Secondary. The extremely high primary pupil:teacher ratio of 63:1
(para 2.27) compares very unfavorably with a median for sub-Sahran Africa of
39:1. 4/ Most classrooms are not designed for more than 50 pupils, and severe
overcrowding detracts from learning. As noted in para 2.27, Government would
like to reduce this ratio to 55sl. On the other hand, there is scope for
increasing student:teacher ratios in secondary schools and the University,
where they are only 21:1 and 10:1 respectively, low by African standards.
Modest increases in these ratios would not compromise quality, but would allow
increases in enrollments without large investments in new facilities.
2.41      Teacher Under-utilization in Secondary and Post-Secondary.  The
qualification required of secondary school teachers is a University Degree or
Diploma. These programs prepare them to teach one subject and this restricts
their use in fields outside their specialization. Consequently, many teachers
are under-employed with an average teaching load of 25-26 periods a week out
of 45 time-tabled periods. Although students at both JC and MCE levels are
required to take eight prescribed core subjects and up to three optional
subjects out of 15, in practice the number of subjects offered in any school
is determined by available staff and physical resources. In a majority of
cases, school facilities tend to be unused beyond mid-afternoon. The lower
4/ IBRD, Education in sub-Saharan Africa, p.40.



- 20 -
enrollment ratio at this level end the need to reduce the number of subjects
offered suggest that schools could be better utilized through double shifting
(paras 3.29 and 3.34).
2.42      Unit Costs Need to be Contained throuah Efficiency Improvements.
Costs per graduating student depend on unit costs and on internal efficiency,
measured by dropout and repetition rates. Containment of these costs will
depend largely on improvements in quality and internal efficiency which reduce
the number of student years per graduate, and at post-primary levels, on
raising student: teacher ratios. At the primary level, Government plans to
lower this ratio from its present undesirably high level of 63t1 will place
upward pressure on unit costs. There is a need to counteract this by
efficiency gains and to complement it with quality improvements. Salary
levels are obviously an important determinant of unit costs. (Wage costs are
about 50 percent of total primary and secondary level recurrent costs and
ebout 40 percent of teacher training and University costs.) Teachers' salary
scales were increased by an average of 40 percent in 1986. This did not fully
compensate for the erosion of real wages by the high rates of 'nflation, which
have persisted, and salary scales were again adjusted in early 1989. Unit
costs (excluding annualized capital costs) and enrollments for various levels
of the education system are shown in Annex Table 3.
2.43      Access to University is Low: University Reforms have Responded to
Labor Market Needs. Access to university education is very limited. In 1987,
there were places for only 600 of the 3,500 students who passed the MCE
examination. In 1989 there were even fewer such places because of a reduced
number of dropouts. A small expansion in numbers was achieved by admitting
the first set of day students. Given the cost burden of board and lodging,
further expansion must be through increasing the number of non-residential
students. In response to recommendations by the Bank and in other reports
including a 1987-88 UNESCO review of Higher Education in Malawi, the
iaiversity has made considerable improvements in efficiency, organization, and
management, and has adjusted the numbers admitted to different courses in
response to the demand for graduates. Since 1983/84, enrollment in the
sciences has almost doubled and in the humanities has been reduced by almost
half (para 3.25). Specialized courses in agriculture and engineering have
been introduced in response to employer requirements.
F. Management. Planning. Research. Evaluation. Institutional and
Organizational Issues
2.44      The Ministry of Education: Decentralization Would Improve MOEC
Management. Although the Ministry of Education employs 33 percent of civil
servants and manages about 11 percent of the national budget, it is not
equipped to use these resources effectively. In April 1988, the Education
Service Review (ESR) was completed. Of all the subjects covered by the ESR,
the Ministmry's organization and management style receives the most exhaustive
and detailed attention. The ESR finds several key problem areas: (a)
excessive centralization and concentration of decision making - even for
routine operational matters at high Ministry levels in Lilongwe: (b) poor1y-
defined work objectives, authority and responsibility at all levels; (c)
excessive hierarchial layers; (d) a need to strengthen all aspects of career
development. The ESR recommendations follow "modern management theory": (a)



- 21 -
decentralize geographically and rationalize decision-making so that high
levels of authority can concentrate on policy; (b) define iork objectives and
base performance reviews on achievement; (c) restructure the organization; (d)
imp3ament a series of actions to enhance career development. The ESR observes
that many of the recommendations would change the Ministry's management
culture and might therefore encounter resistance. While the Ministry accepts
the ESR recommendations in principle, doubt remains as to whether It will put
them into effect (Annex D).
2.45      Planning System Needs StrengtheninR.  Planning weaknesses have a
particularly serious impact on the sector and its future, and stem from
understaffing of the planning department, separation of the planning and
administrative functions, and insufficient planning guidelines. Effective
management and planning have been made even more difficult by budgetary
uncertainties. While the decision to involve district levels in the planning
process should be commended, a mechanism for consolidating district plans and
giving them operational effect should be established at both regional and
headquarters levels. While MOEC has prepared a Ten Year Plan (to 1994195), it
suffers from a number of deficiencies, the most important of which are (a) a
lack of prioritization of objectives, (b) the lack of year-by-year targets,
and consequently, of the means to monitor performance or adjust targets, (c)
the scarcity of quantitative targets, whether in terms of people or in terms
of costs, and (d) little focus on the m2eans of implementation. The planning
process at MOEC, in fact, appears largely a derivative of funds availability:
from Treasury for recurrent costs, from donors for development. To improve
this process, MOEC at the urging of the Bank is preparing a year-by-year (to
1991/92) demand-driven (i.e. what the Ministry proposes) itemization of
quantitative targets (e.g. numbers of students, teachers and classrooms),
prioritization of policy changes, and estimates of recurrent costs for all
aspects of education (the University would prepare its own plan, to accompany
the MOEC Plan (para 3.35)).
2.46      Stevs Have Been Taken to Improve Research and Evaluation.  Supported
by the First Education Sector Credit (Credit 1767-MAI), the research and
evaluation unit of the MOEC was expanded. The arrangement under which the
Unit is located in the Ministry has proved unsatisfactory because of staffing
constraints, competing claims on tbe time of the research staff and unwieldy
bureaucratic procedures. After much discussion, MOEC has taken steps to
improve the situation and a decision was made to move the unit to the
University, as this is a more appropriate research environment. The Center
for Education Research at the University would have a strong policy
orientation and much of the research undertaken would be in response to
proposals from MOEC (paras 3.35 and 4.16).
G. Bank Group Experience and Lessons Learned
2.47      The Bank has been active in the education and training sector since
1963 as reflected in six education projects (two of which are ongoing) and in
sector work -- particularly the study on Financing and Efficiency of
Education, (Report No. 4894-MAI) and policy discussions which resulted in the
preparation of the Second Education Plan. The Bank has also undertaken a
Human Resource Development Study which takes a longer term perspective, and
financed a management review of the MOEC.



- 22 -
2.48      Bank lending in the sector has focused on reinforcement of general
education at the primary and secondary levels. The four completed projects
supported expansion of and quality improvements at the primary and secondary
levels including cechnical education. The First Education Project (Credit
102-MAI, signed in 1967) provided US$6.3 million equivalent to expand
secondary education to meet manpower needs, to introduce technical subjects in
secondary schools, and to establish a new primary teacher training college in
Lilongwe. A Project Performance Audit Report (No. 2914) was completed in
March, 1980. The Second Education Project (Credit 590-MAI, signed in 1975)
provided US$11.6 million equivalent to establish a new primary teacher
training college in Mzuzu, to expand secondary education, principally through
provision of additional places for girls, and to establish 23 model primary
schools which would also serve as rural adult education centers. A project
completion report dated September 21, 1982 has been distributed. The project
was not formally audited. The Third Education Project (Credit 910-MAI and EEC
Credit 12-MAI, signed in 1979 and totalling US$22.0 million) expanded and
broadened the Bank Group's involvement in educational development. It was
designed to improve (a) primary education through self-help construction of
1000 primary school classrooms and provision of textbooks to primary school
children; (b) educational management, through establishment of the Malawi
Institute of Education (MIE), provision of vehicles for the school
inspectorate, and support for various educational studies and evaluation; and
(c) training of essential middle level manpower, through expansion of the MCDE
(then known as Malawi Correspondence College) and the Chancellor College
secondary teacher training program, as well as through modest expansion of
agricultural, commercial, and general secondary education. The Credit Account
was closed on December 31st, 1985. A Project Completion Report dated August,
1988 has been distributed. The Fourth Education Project (Credit 1123-MAI,
signed May, 1981 for US$41.0 million equivalent) provided for 3,960 new
secondary student places and for further expansion of Chancellor College's
secondary teacher training program. The project also included pre-investment
studies for a new Institute of Public Administration and a new primary teacher
training college, as well as funds to prepare a National Educational Plan.
The Credit Account was closed December 31st, 1986 and a Project Completion
Report dated August, 1988 has been distributed.
2.49      Evaluation of these four completed projects indicates inter alia
good success in assisting the Government to increase access to primary
education and to improve the quality of primary school teachers. At the
secondary level, implementation of the four projects has resulted in
enrollment increases as planned and the secondary schools are well managed..
Both the Third and Fourth Education projects assisted in increasing the number
of qualified secondary school teachers. The introduction of technical
subjects, especially wood and metal work, into secondary schools has not been
fully satisfactory because of high recurrent costs, lack of specialized
teachers and lack of interest. Because of these problems, the MOEC is no
longer introducing technical subjects into general secondary schools.
Experience with the self-help primary school scheme under the Third Project
was unsatisfactory because of management and organizational problems. An
important lesson learned is that communities are overburdened with self-help
schemes in every sector, and consequently considerable lead time for
implementation of self-help schemes may be required.  Therefore, the self-help
component must be limited to items that are easily available, such as bricks,
and should not include additional community labor inputs.



- 23 -
2.50      The Fifth Education Pro1ect (Credit 1330-MAI signed May 5, 1983, to
provide SDR 30.9 million) was designed to improve primary education, expand
the output of secondary level educational institutions, increase the number of
trained accountants and accounting technicians and to strengthen the education
management and auditing systems. Apart from a slight delay in providing
counterpart funds, overall implementation of the project is satisfactory. The
project will close September 30, 1990.
2.51      The First Education Sector Credit (Credit 1767-MAI signed May 7,
1987 and totalling US$27.0 million) is designed as a sector credit and its
objectives are to assist in carrying out the first phase of the Second Ten-
Year Education Plan (1984/5 - 199415) and restructuring the sector to improve
quality, equity and efficiency and management, all in accordance with a Policy
Action Program which is part of the legal agreemezt.  Subject to modifications
upon which the Borrower and the Association may agree from time to time to
achieve the objectives, the Credit finances (a) construction, furnishing and
equipping about 1,500 primary classrooms, 750 primary teacher houses, two
secondary boarding schools and one secondary day school, (b) expansion and
upgrading of three MCDE centers, and the Fational Examination Center, (c)
provision of about two million basic student textbooks, (d) improvement of in-
service training for primary school teachers, primary school supervision, MCDE
organization and operation and MOEC's management, (e) implementation of cost
recovery measures at secondary schools and university, (f) establishment of a
loan scheme for university students, and (g) studies. The policies are being
implemented and the overall implementation is satisfactory (see Annex H for
the status of implementation), although budget cuts have ca :sed delays. The
Project is expected to be completed by the original Closing Date. The
proposed Second Education Sector Credit furthers the objectives of this
project.
H.  Role of Other International Asencies and Non-Government Ortanizations
2.52      Although the Bank Group has been the major source of external
financing in the sector, a number of other sources of assistance have made
vital contributions of various kinds. USAID through the Human Resources
Institutional Development (HRID) project is assisting the Government in
graduate level training. USAID has also assisted in the development of the
Polytechnic. ODA has provided (a) fellowships for specialized training in the
United Kingdom, (b) a limited number of English, Math, and Science teachers at
the secondary level; (c) assistance with the development of technical schools
and (d) managerial assistance for the MCDE. UNICEF has assisted with
upgrading of untrained primary school teachers. UNDP as well as CIDA have
financed equipment and technical assistance for curriculum development at MIE.
UNDP has recently reached an understanding with MOEC to provide technical
assistance and fellowships for the planning function of MOEC. African
Development Bank has funded two education projects which support construction
of (a) primary schools in the urban areas, (b) secondary schools and (c)
offices for REOs. NGOs: Malawi has a tradition of missionary involvement in
secondary education. Most grant-aided schools are church affiliated schools.



- 24 -
III. GOVERNMENT STRATEGY AND ACTION PLAN
3.01      To address the issues discussed in Chapter II and work toward
Its objectives for education as outlined in Chapter I, the Government has
developed and committed itself to a strategy and action plan involving (a)
an updating of its public expenditure plan for the remainder of the
1985186-1994/95 planning period and (b) an associated policy reform
program. A brief overview of the key features of the reform program and
public expenditure plan Is presented in Sections A and B, and then further
details are outlined, issue by issue, in subsequent sections. The strategy
and action plan embodied in the public expenditure and reform packages are
the foundation for the proposed Credit described in Chapter IV.
A. Key Features of the Reform Protram and
Updated Public Expenditure Plan
3.02      The reform program was developed through an iterative,
consultative process, taking into account (a) the Government objectives in
the sector, (b) the requirements of the sector, (c) resource constraints,
{d) implementation constraints, and (e) the feasibility of possible
changes, considering consensus-building and political economy issues. The
reform program agenda is summarised in a detailed list and timetable of
agreed actions to i,e taken, as set forth in a Letter of Education
Development Policy with an attached Policy Action Program in the form of a
matrix of specific objectives and measures. This letter, signed by the
Minister of Finance on behalf of the Government and in consultation with
the MOEC, is included as Annex A, with the matrix attached (para 6.01).
3.03      The reform package constitutes a serious attempt by the
Government to address the principal problems and issues in the sector, as
outlined in Chapter II. It represents a continuation of the changes
initiated in conjunction with the First Education Sector Credit. The
Government has used the forum of the National Advisory Council on Education
to discuss the most controversial aspects of the policies in order to build
a consensus. As part of the First Education Sector Credit, the Government
has also set up a monitoring and evaluation committee to ensure that the
program of reform is implemented. To date, several significant measures
have been carried out (Annex A, Attachment 1).
3.04      The reform package as a whole, when fully implemented, is
expected to improve:
(a) quality, by improving facilities, deploying more trained
teachers and providing more and better textbooks;
(b) access and equity, by providing more primary schools and
reducing fees in the first four years of primary school and
increasing the number of secondary school places;



- 25 -
(c) efficiency, by reducing primary dropout and repetition rates,
and by making more use intensive use of facilities and teachers
at the secondary levell
(d) resource allocation and mobilization, by incre:sing the share of
education resources devoted to the primary level and adjusting
relative cost recovery at different levels;
(e) education management, through decentralization of education
services and improvements in the planning process.
3.05      The updated expenditure plan consists of approved targets for
investments and recurrent spending by the central Government for the
education sector for the remainder of the ten-year period from 1985/86
through 1994195, after which time a follow-on ten-year plan will be phased
in. The investment portion of the Plan is summarized in Table 3.1 and the
recurrent portion in Table 3.2. As with the reform progra&z, these
expenditure plans were developed and agreed to through an iterative,
consultative process that took into account (a) the requirements of the
sector, (b) the resource constraints, (c) the reform process, past and
future, and its expected effects on both th* requirements and the
constraints, (d) the findings and implications of the Public Expenditure
Review covering the entire Government budget, (e) the planning done
previously on education expenditures (especially in the 1985 - 1987 period,
when the FiLst Education Sector Credit was being prepared) and the lessons
of experience from those efforts, in the light of later evidence on how
well the plans were achieved, and (f) the prospects for the overall
economic and budgetary picture in the next five to seven years.  The
expenditure plan and the reform program are, by design and of necessity,
closely inter.inked. The expenditure plan supports and facilitates the
reforms, and v.ce versa.  Each depends for its success on the satisfactory
progress of the other.
B. Financina
3.06      The Level of Total Public Expenditure on Education.  To develop
the expenditure plan summarized in Tables 3.1 and 3.2, a review and
assessment were done of alternative programs and investment options, in
relation to the agreed objectives for the sector, and what could feasibly
be done in the remaiLder of the 1985/86 - 1994/95 period to move closer
toward those objectives. Building upon work carried out initially for the
Public Expenditure Review, estimates were prepared for different scenarios.
3.07      These calculations, as in all projections for future events,
required decisions and assumptions about how certain crucial policy choices
will be resolved and how other factors beyond Government control will
evolve. The principal assumptions underlying the final expenditure plan
that has been adopted relate to:
-    the growth in enrollment rates
-    the progress of quality improvement initiatives, particularly in
regard to (a) teachers (numbers, compensation levels, and length



- 26 -
and type of training) and (b) instructional materials
(pupil/textbook ratio, etc.)
-    the success of measures to reduce costs. improve efficiency, and
adjust cost recovery
-    the development of physical facilities (school maintenance, and
standards for classrooms size, furnishings, and quality of
construction)
-    the prospects for raising the level of public expenditure on
education (which depends on what happens to (a) the total budget
and (b) education's share of it)
-    the prospects for mobilizing additional resources from other
sources (community self-help, fees, donor assistance).
The stance taken on each of these dimensions, and reasons involved, are
discussed in the "issues" sections following. In each case, tradeoffs had
to be made betweenw .t would be desirable considering the needs and what
is possible considering the resource constraints.
