3.08      The expenditure plan from the First Educativn Sector Credit
provided a convenient benchmark to start from. A revised plan reflecting
the new circumstances was arrived at more easily and effectively because
the consultative process was able to focus on where the earlier plan needed
updating and refining.
3.09      When the expenditure plan was first formulated, it seemed fully
fundable and consistent with the then-expected budgetary picture. It
envisaged that: primary school enrollment would grow at 5.8 percent per
annum; annual enrollment growth at the university would be limited to 4
percent; and all other plan targets would be met. The total investment
package for the ten year pe-iod was estimated at MK349.2 in current prices,
63.3 percent of the original proposal in the Education Plan. The savings
were achieved by relying on community self-help for primary school
construction, by building day secondary schools without expensive boarding



- 27 -
facilities and by reducing the university growth target. The Appraisal
Report recommended that if further cuts were imposed by resource
limitations, these should not be directed at primary education, but at post
primary levels, particularly the university and technical/vocational
training.
3.10      There have been two primary developments that make a further
revision of that plan necessary. First, the preliminary results of the
1987 census show a much larger population than had been projected on the
basis of the 1977 census. The population appears to have grown at an
annual rate of 3.5 percent between 1977 and 1987. This means that progress
towards meeting the enrollment rate targets by 1994/95 will require much
greater expansion of the school system than planned. Second, considerable
deterioration of the economic situation in 1986 necessitated severe cuts in
Government expenditure in 1987/88 and in the budgets for subsequent years.
Instead of the MK146 million (current prices) anticipated for education
sector investments for the three year period 1987/88-1989/90, MK 74 was
made available - only 51 percent. Higher cost escalation than anticipated
has further retarded realization of the investment plan.
3.11      Lower cost options have to be adopted if the targets for primary
school enrollment and the pupil$teacher ratio are to be met. Even the
primary schools being built under the First Education Sector Credit (whose
capital costs are half those of the schools being built under the ongoing
AfDB project) are unaffordable given present budget constraints and the
very large number of new classrooms needed (paras 2.34 and 3.28). With
communities providing bricks and the project financing providing other
building materials and construction by small local contractors, costs are
MK23,000 per rural classroom and MK56,000 per urban classroom. Government
has little option but to rely more heavily on community contributions and
construction. A major problem with past self-help construction has been
the inability of communities to buy roofing sheets, the most expensive
component. Through the proposed Credit, Government will supply roofing
sheets for 228 unroofed classrooms and 406 teachers' houses (par& 4.12).
3.12      New primary teacher traiaing colleges would have a capital cost
of approximately MK30,000 per traitse place, and an annual recurrent cost
per trainee of MK1,165 (1988/89) or MK2,330 per graduate (Annex Table 3).
An in-service training course using distance teaching methods could be
developed at a cost of MK4.42 million, with total recurrent costs per
graduate of as little as MK830 (exclusive of trainee salaries) (Annex B and
para 4.12). This would provide a flexible, low cost training option which
could be of comparable quality to the present pre-service full-time teacher
training. It would not entail high-cost investment in new capacity which
might no longer be needed onco enough new teachers had been trained to
lower the pupil:teachex ratio to the desired level.
3.13      Achieving the expected educational gains from investments in
facilities, training and other inputs depends on the strength of
management, administration and planning of the education sector. In
recognition of this, the development budget makes provision for investments
to strengthen and Improve these functions.



-      -
3.14      The new (i.e., updated) Plan for the remainder of the 1985186 -
1994/95 period has been structured with the above points in mind.
3.15      The investment budget, as Table 3.1 indicates, will be allocated
in the 1990/91 - 1994/95 period as follows: 37 percent on expanding access
and improving quality at the primary level, 24 percent for secondary
education (including MCDE), 18 percent for primary school teachers'
training, 4 percent for tertiary education, and 17 percent for improvements
in the inspectorate, the examinations system, and Ministry planning,
management and administration. This will not entirely meet the expansion
goals in the Ten-Year Education Plan: there is no provision for the 30 new
secondary schools included in the Education Plan, and only 10 new MCDE
centers are included. The MOEC has decided on priority investments which
could be added to the Investment Plan if additional financing becomes
available in later years of the Plan. During negotiation, agreement was
reached with the Government that IDA's concurrence would te sought for any
changes in the investment program (para 6.06 (a)).
3.16      The Education Share of the Government Budget.  The education
sector recurrent expenditure requirements increasingly have been
underfunded as new investments have expanded the educational system. 71  In
order for the potential returns on educational investments to be realized,
adequate financial resources must be available to the sector to support the
quantitative and qualitative improvements planned. Projections of the
recurrent expenditures required to support the planned (and ongoing)
investments in the education system have been estimated to the end of the
Plan period, comparing a hypothetical scenario without reforms under which
many of the Plan targets remain unattainable, and a scenario "with
reforms'. These projections are then compared with estimates of the
resources likely to be available to the education sector. Table 3.2 shows
the increase in net expenditures required to achieve the revised plan
targets (detailed assumptions and projection data are given in Annex Table
4). The updated recurrent expenditure plan provides for an increase in
education's share that is consistent with the Government objectives and
commitment as set forth in Devpol, and with the conclusions of the Public
Expenditure Review. In this, it represents a major step forward, in accord
with national policy and in keeping with overall positions on resource
utilization. Education's share would rise significantly from its 11.1X
level in 1988189. The increase to the 15 percent target in Devpol by
1994/95 is essential if the reform program is to be implemented
successfully and the Plan targets achieved. Even the scenario without
reforms, which falls far short of achieving the Plan goals for expansion
and quality improvements, would require an increase in education's share of
the total Government revenue budget to 13.4 percent by 1994/95. Moreover,
the projections make the conservative assumption that wages and salaries of
MOEC employees maintain their 1988/89 real levels, unlike the projection of
total Government expenditure which builds in a gradual restoration of

1987 and 1988 has eroded the gains of the 1986 salary revision,  and

personnel in the teaching force. Without a sizeable increase in resource
allocation to education, access to basic education will be denied to
thousands of Malawian children and the quality of the Malawian educational
system will be seriously jeopardized.

3.17      Table 3.3 explores the implications of different assumptions about
resource availability. For the purpose of this exp-sition, it is assumed that
the impact of a resource shortfall is on the size (but not quality) of the
primary level. It is more realistic to expect that both quality and expansion
will be affected to some extent. Without any increase in its share of
Government recurrent expenditure, the education budget grows at 3.7 percent in
real terms, which would barely allow the system to keep pace with the growth
of the school-age population. If post-pzimary budgetary levels were not cut
and the plenned quality improvements were implemented at the primary level,
then the budget for primary schools and teacher training would not be adequate
to support even the present total enrollment, and the gross enrollment rate
would fall substantially. A modest increase in the education share to 13
percent would allow a very small gain in the gross enrollment rate, from 55
percent to 58, or to 59 if the additional revenues from increased fee levels
at post-primary levels were allocated to the primary level sub-budget.



- 31 -
3.18      Allocations Within the Education Budget.  The updated plan will
result in a gradual reallocation of public expenditure on education in
directions consistent with the Government's policies. Primary education will
receive enhanced emphasis, fulfilling aims identified long ago. The
reallocation will affect both the investment and the recurrent budgets.
3.19      The recurrent budget (Table 3.2) will change considerably, resulting
by 1994/95 in a substantially larger proportion for primary education than has
been the pattern until now. The share for primary will increase from 52
percent in 1988189 to 62 percent in 1994/95.
3.20      An increased resource allocation allows primary enrollments to grow
to 1.73 million, the pupil:teacher ratio to fall to 55:1, and other quality-
enhancing measures to be taken, notably increased provision for textbooks.
The proposed special in-service teachers' training course would allow a much
lower cost, more rapid expansion in the number of trained teachers than would
be feasible relying on full-time pre-service residential courses. At the
secondary level, the planned increase in the pupil:teacher ratio from 21 to 25
and introduction of double shifts will allow 27 percent more formal secondary
school places to be provided than without these changes, without a
commensurate increase in recurrent costs.  Average recurrent expenditure per
student is projected to fall, although this will be contingent in part on
nominal fee increases that at least maintain the real level of cost reccvery
at this level. At the university, continued efficiency improvements and
increasing numbers of day students will result in modest recurrent unit cost
reductions. The reduction in real per capita expenditures for
technical/vocational education is the result of anticipated efficiency
improvements. An overall real annual growth of recurrent expenaiture of 8.8
percent is required, with expenditures for the primary school and teacher
training subsectors growing faster, and expenditures for post-secondary
education and the MCDE growing more modestly.
3.21      Cost Recovery and Other Sour-:es of Financing.  In deciding on
appropriate fees at each level of the system, unmet demand and the possible
additional number of places that could be financed out of additional revenues
must be weighed against people's ability to pay and the likely impact of fee
changes on attendance and access for those from low income families. Cost
recovery levels are very low at post-primary levels (except for MCDE). The
long waiting lists for places in the few much higher cost private secondary
schools suggests that at least some parents are both willing and able to pay
considerably higher fees than presently charged. Moreover, the high inflation
rates of recent years have greatly eroded the real level of school fees. The
proposed increase in secondary tuition fees to K60 in 1992/93 will restore
them to only their 1986/87 real level (para 6.05(b)). University students
have begun to pay a net tuition fee equal to only 3 percent of costs.
3.22      The Government budget currently provides over 90 percent of all
education recurrent financing. The key determinants of resource availability
for the education sector are the growth rates of GDP and of the overall
Government revenue budget, and the share of the revenue budget that is
allocated to education. Fees and other income generated within the education
sector are an important supplementary source of revenue for recurrent
expenditures. The most recent projection of real GDP growth is 3.7 percent



- 32 -
for 1989190, accelerating gradually to 5 percent in 1993/94 and 1994/95.
Total Government expenditure (comprising interest, the development and revenue
budgets and extrabudgetary expenditures) is expected to grow in real terms at
3.9 percent, and the overall Government revenue budget to grow at a slightly
lower rate of 3.4 percent to reach MK1108.5 million in 1994/95 (1988
prices) 91, compared to a (revised) estimate for 1988/89 of MK907.0 million.
3.23      The proposed increases in user charges for University students would
increase cost recovery from 3 to 7 percent. The proposed increase in
secondary level fees would increase cost recovery in day schools from 8
percent to 17 percent, and in boarding schools from 20 percent to 30 percent
of 1989 costs, although inflation and other cost increases will result in a
smaller real cost recovery increase (Table 4.2 and para 6.02(i) and 6.04(c)).
These are to be complementeJ by other measures designed to reduce unit costs
such as more intensive use of physical facilities at the secondary and higher
educational levels, improved education planning and increased teacher loads at
the secondary level.
C. QualitY
3.24      To improve the quality of education and consequent student
achievement, Government's strategy calls for: (a) an increased number of
qualified teachers at both primary and secondary levels; (b) increased school
inputs including textbooks and Improved secondary level library resources;
(c) curriculum revision and development including preparation of supporting
instructional materials at all levels; (d) expanded primary school
inspectorate system; and (e) a revised test and measurement system to reflect
the curriculum chAnges.
3.25      Teachers.  The Government intends to increase the supply of
qualified primary teachers by expanding existing teacher training colleges and
by building additional colleges. The present teacher training colleges will
enable an estimated 1,700 new teachers to graduate each year. Allowing for an
attrition rate of 1 percent, the present training capacity would result in
about 24,500 primary school teachers by 1994/95. This would be adequate to
teach 1.641 million pupils with a pupiltteacher ratio of 67:1, or 1.347
million with a lower ratio of 55:1. Meeting target enrollment and
pupilsteacher ratios will require an increase in the supply of trained
teachers beyond the present PTTC capacity. Investment in new teacher training
facilities would be difficult given the current fiscal situation. Other
programs designed to improve the quality of the teaching force include an in-
service training program for unqualified teachers supported by UNICEF,
completion of the review of the curriculum of teacher training colleges, and
upgrading courses for qualified teachers and headmasters. Quantitative and
qualitative Improvements in school supervision and in primary teacher
education are also planned. There is a shortage of secondary school science
teachers. To makA up for the thortfall in the number of nationals qualified
to teach science subjects, the Government has been relying on technical
9/ Based on the less constrained scenario of the "Policy Framework Paper",
IBRD Consultative Group document MAL88-2, June 6, 1988.



- 33 -
assistance. The Plan envisages a substantial increase in the number of
student places available for those majoring in science. Since 1983184,
enrollment in the sciences at the university has almost doubled (para 2.43).
In addition, the Government has revised and consolidated the primary
curriculum and intends to strengthen the broadcasting system including
developing effective new programs (para 2.23).
3.26      Textbooks.  The Government plans to revise most textbooks.  Most of
the revised instructional materials will be developed by a small group of
writers to be drawn from subject committees. During each writers workshop,
the writers will receive training on how to prepare a pupil's book and
teacher's guide. Much of the work will be completed at the workshop. In some
cases, the writing will extend beyond the workshop. The process of developing
the instructional materials will include careful review of the documents at
each stage. Outside consultants providid by UNDP and IDA will assist with
this review. An important part of the process of developing the material is
fcrmative evaluation, an evaluation process that seeks to improve the
materials as they are being created. As the instructional materials are being
developed, they will be tested in selected schools near MIE. A much broader
but less intensive formative evaluation will be carried out during the second
year for each standard. Feedback will be obtained mostly through
questionnaires.
D. Access to Educational O* ortunitiea
3.27      The targets for expansion of educat'.onal facilities formulated five
years ago for the Education Plan now seem unattainable in the light of
resource constraints and the new population census data. Although these
targets have not been formally revised, MOEC recognizes that they will take
longer to accomplish than originally expected.
3.28      Primary level.  The Government's objective is to ensure that by the
end of the Plan period 85 percent of the age group (6-13) are enrolled in
school. This target is based on population projections using the 1977 census
data, which suggest that a net enrollment rate of 85 percent in 1994/95 would
imply a total enrollment of 1.4 million. Revised projections using the newly
available 1987 data indicate that there will be 2.356 million 6-13 year olds
in 1994/95, 85 percent of which would be 2.003 million. In 1986/87, 1.022
million children were enrolled. An enrollment of 2.003 million in 1994195 at
the present pupilsclassroom ratio of 70:1 would require an additional 14,013
classrooms to be constructed. Lowering this ratio to the desired 55:1 would
mean an additional 21,818 classrooms, equivalent to constructing about 3,600
new classrooms each remaining year of the Plan period. On-going IDA and AFDB
projects will add about 2,500 classrooms, an average of 417 classrooms a year.
The investment cost of the additional 3,183 classrooms each year would be
about MK89 million - about three times the current annual development budget
of the Ministry. Given the recent implementation difficulties and the
scarcity of funds, the Government has scaled down its plan considerably and
intends to implement an expanded self-help primary school school construction
program (para 3.11).



- 34 -
3.29      *econdary level.  In 1986/87, there were 26,000 formal secondary
school places, 8,000 short of the planned quantitative target of 34,000 which
would provide places for 3.7 percent of the 14-17 year old cohort in 1994/95.
Achieving the desired enrollment rate of 5 percent would require 46,000 places
or 20,000 additional places.  On-going IDA and AFDB projects will provide
6,900 additional neu- places. The Government intends meeting its target by
making more intentive use of existing facilities: (a) increasing the
pupil$teacher ratio from its current level of 21:1 to 25:1 uthich would
increase enrollment by 5,580 in 1994/95; (b) by double shifts in urban schools
(Annex E); and (c) by making more intensive use of staff through increasing
teaching hours. The Government has completed a secondary school mapping plan
which indicates that day secondary schools are a feasible alternative to the
current system of boarding. Therefore, substantial investment in new
secondary schools, especially day schools, is planned. Furthermore, MOEC will
encourage private secondary schools development and self-help community
construction of secondary schools. To ensure equal access to senior secondary
school, MOEC will revert to a merit based selection policy for Form III and
maintain accurate statistics to enable the policy to be monitored.
Additionally, Government plans to construct about 30 new day secondary schools
with a total enrollment of 7,200. The new policies of a second shift and
increasing the proportion of day students entail a slight change in admission
criteria so that boarding facilities will be reserved for students whose homes
are not in the immediate vicinity of the school (para 2.41). The Plan
anticipates that MCDE would enroll about 44,000 students by 1994/95. On the
assumption that each study center serves about 200 students, this would
require the construction of an additional 100 centers by the end of the Plan
period. It also implies an annual enrollment increase of 10 percent.
Historically, MCDE enrollment has been growing at 2.8 percent per annum. It
therefore seems unlikely that it would reach 44,000 by 1994/95.
3.30      Primary Teacher Trainina ColleRes.  The Government plans to fully
utilize existing facilities and introduce changes in selection procedures to
ensure that colleges are filled within two weeks of commencement of the
academic year. Furthermore, the Government plans to train about 4,500
teachers in a special training program (Annex B).
3.31      Technical/Vocational Education.  Government's plan is to provide a
total of 440 technician level places, 980 staff training places, 400
commercial and business study places and 380 places for assistant craftsmen
and specialist courses. However, the capacity expansion originally included
in the Plan and in Devpol is not justified while capacity utilization remains
low and growth of formal sector employment sluggish.
3.32      Tertiary  Education.  The Plan for the university provides for
enrollment to double to 4,000 full time student equivalents by 1995, including
500 students in an external studies department and 200 in the proposed Faculty
of Medicine. In recognition of the budgetary implications of a new medical
school, the Government has revised the phasing of the development of a full
scale medical school and is now planning to begin with one year of clinical
training in country while continuing to train medical students abroad.
Although the Education Plan originally envisaged considerable new investment
in the university, it has been agreed that this be postponed, since some
increase in student numbers can be achieved without new investments, and the



- 35 -
present growth in employment opportunities does not warrant a large general
increase. The university intends to expand the policy begun in 1988 to admit
day students and complete the feasibility study on a distance education
facility at the university.
S. Efficiency
3.33      The proposed reform package constitutes a serious attempt by the
Government to tackle the quality and efficiency issues at the primary level
(paras 2.39-2.40). Multiple repetition of grades, especially standard eight,
is not unusual. Placing a limit on the number of times a child repeats a
grade has been politically difficult for the Government. The new strategy
will (a) discourage repetition especially in standard eight by reserving 75
percent of all Form I places for those who pass the PSLC examination on the
first attempt and registering all primary school students to prevent pupils
transferring to new schools undetected; (b) reduce repetition in the lower
grades through developing new teaching techniques, including making effective
use of school radio broadcasting; (c) reduce the number of dropouts by
abolishing school fees at standards 1 through 4 and substantially increasing
school inputs at this level.
3.34      The studentsteacher ratio at the secondary level is to be increased
to 25:1, through increased workloads for teachers who will be required to
teach an average of 33 periods weekly with a minimum of 27 contact hours
(paras 2.40 and 2.41). As part of its agreement with IDA under the First
Sector Credit (Credit 1767-MAI) the Government is phasing out construction of
boarding facilities and has increased boarding fees in secondary schools. As
part of the agreement for the proposed new Credit, assurance will be received
that boarding and tuition fees will be increased further (para 6.02 (i)). In
order fully to utilize existing technical/vocational facilities, the
Government is transferring the Board of Governors' assistant technician
training courses from the Polytechnic to the technical colleges. The
university has taken steps to improve efficiency, management and organization
(para 2.43). It has adjusted the numbers admitted to various courses in
response to market demand, reduced its administrative costs substantially and
eliminated the allowances paid to students. It charges a net tuition fee of
MK200 and has in place a loan scheme for indigent students. The university at
present only recovers about 3 percent of its running costs compared to 15
percent at the secondary level (including MCDE). The Government needs to
increase further the user charges at the university, and plans to raise
university fees to MK300 in the 1992/93 academic year and MK400 in 1993/94
(Attachment 1 of Annex A).
F. Manatement. Plannint and Research
3.35      Required actions include: strengthen the inspectorate; reconstitute
the National Advisory Council to include parents and non-educators; strengthen
the planning function within MOEC, regional and district planning, and MIE;



- 36 -
10/ complete and formulate a five-year rolling Education Plans decentralize
the activities of the MOEC Headquarters; prepare a human resource development
strategy; institutionalize the monitoring and evaluation procedure; grant
autonomy to MCDE in regard to financing and staffing; review the sa,ary
structure of accounting and specialized course instructors as a means of
retaining such persons and strengthen the education research and evaluation
capacity of the university (paras 2.45-2.46).
G. Impacts and Viability/Sustainability of the Protram
3.36      Educational Impact.  The policy reforms described above will have
positive impacts on the quality of educational services, enrollments at all
levels, and on the efficiency with which education resources are used. The
gross primary enrollment rate is expected to increase from 58 to 73 percent by
1994/95 compared to a negligible improvement under the status quo (see Table
3.4). The most dramatic increase is in the gross enrollment ratio for girls
which is expected to increase from 50 to 70 percent by 1994/95 compared with
54 percent without reform (Annex Table 5). The reforms will result in
increases in the numbers who enter standard 1 and in the number of students
who remain in school. New intakes into standard 1 are expected to grow by 5.6
percent per annum compared to 3.9 percent without reform. Significant
improvements in etudent flows in all primary standards are expected. Dropout
and repetition rates are expected to drop considerably (Charts 4 and 5). The
number of graduates per 1,000 entrants is expected to increase from 364 in
1986187 to 749 in 1994/95 for boys and 296 in 1986/87 to 730 for girls in
1994/95. The number of students completing primary education within only 8
years is expected to increase ten times. The reduction in dropout and
repetition rates will lower by almost 50 percent the average number of years
it takes to produce a graduate (Annex Table 6). The most significant
improvement is expected in standard 8 where repetition is expected to drop
considerably from 48 percent to about 10 percent as a result of improvements
in the examination system, the new registration policy, the new selection
procedure to Form I and expanded secondary access. Enrollment in formal
secondary schools is expected to grow at 4.8 percent per annum, while
enrollment at MCDE and at the university is expected to grow at about 4
percent per annum.

3.37     The enrollment targets presented in para 3.36 have been set within
the context of the physical and human resources available or likely to be
available during the remainder of the planned period. Although enrollment
targets especially at the primary level are expected to be met, improvements
in the classroom:pupil ratio would require considerable success by Government
in mobilizing self-help efforts to construct classrooms especially in the
urban areas where overcrowding is severe. Improving the teacher:pupil ratio
will come through the expanded teacher training program which the proposed
Credit will support.  The success of these policy measures will be
periodically assessed by the Monitoring and Evaluation Committee established
under the First Education Sector Credit which supported the first phase of the
reform process (para 4.29). During this second phase of the reform process
new pedagogical practices would be introduced and improvements in education
flows and use of resources would be made.



- 38 -
3.38       Financial Impact.  Budgetary requirements under the current trends
and with policy changes have been estimated through detailed cost projections
using constant 1988 prices and the 1988/89 budget as the base (Table 3.5).
The projected total recurrent costs in 1994/95 are MK 132.4 million compared
with MK 120.0 without the reform. Most of the increase is required for
quality improvements and additional expansion at the primary level, including
the salary costs of the extra teachers needed to reduce the pupil:teacher
ratio to 55sl. The formal secondary level allocation increase to accommodate
a larger expansion is offset to some extent by the fall in secondary unit
costs due to raising the pupil:teacher ratio and the lower unit costs of a
double shift. The increased student:staff ratio at the university and the
expected increase in fees allow a smaller budgetary allocation. The moderate
expansion expected in technical/vocational education can also be achieved more
cost effectively as a result of efficiency improvements.

3.39        The Education Plan intended that the recurrent budget be
reallocated across the different levels of the system to increase the share
for primary and technical/vocational education, offsetting this by decreases
in the secondary and university shares. Table 3.6 shows the actual
allocations in 1980/81, 1984/85 and 1987/88, the 1988/89 budgeted shares, the
Plan target allocation for 1994/95, and the projected shares given the reform
program. The changes since the beginning of the Plan period are well on trend
towards the 1994/95 goals. Indeed, the planned reallocation in favor of
primary education has already been exceeded. Further overshooting of this
target and of the target share reduction for tertiary education indicated by



- 39 -
the recurrent budget projections under the reform program are consistent with
the educational needs of Malawi and MOEC priorities.

3.40        Cost Effectiveness.  The reform program and investment plan are
expected to improve the cost effectiveness of the system. The expected impact
on unit recurrent costs has been shown in Table 3.2. Although annual unit
costs for primary school pupils rise by 28.6 percent over the period, the
expected reductions in repetition and dropout rates will significantly lower
the cost per primary school graduate, which is a more appropriate measure of
the cost-effectiveness of the system.   The cost per graduate will fall by 27
percent from MK590 to MK430 (in constant prices) as a result of the dramatic
fall in pupil-years per graduate from 16.9 to 9.6.
3.41        Rates of Return.  Estimates of the social rates of return to each
level of education in Malawi using the "short-form" method of analysis 11/
(see Annex Table 1) suggest that investments are indeed justified by the
benefits measured by expected differences in income streams. This conclusion
is strengthened by consideration of the additional benefits which are not
captured by the analysis - improved quality of life, the association between
womens' educational levels and fertility and the nutritional status and health
of their children.
ll/ See G. Psacharopoulos, 1980: Returns to Education, an Updated
International Comparison, in (ed) T. King, Education and Income, World Bank
Staff Working Paper No. 402, July 1980.

IV. THE PROPOSED PJ
A. Obiectives. Rationle-. and Summary Description
4.01      The proposed project would support the Government's efforts to
(1) Implement the strategy and action plan embodied in the public
expenditure and policy reform programs for the sector as outlined in
Chapter II, and thereby (ii) address the issues discussed in Chapter I.
Specifically, the project would contribute to (1) improving the quality of
education at all levels; (2) expanding access and increasing efficiency,
focussing on the primary and secondary levels; (3) strengthening sector
management, budgeting, and planning; and (4) improving resource
mobilization and allocation policies, through increasing cost recovery at
post-primary levels and reversing the decline in the sector's budget share
in accordance with the recommendations of the recent public expenditure
review and with Government's stated policy.
4.02      As noted earlier, the project would assist the Government to
proceed with the second pbase of its long-term educational improvement
plan, building upon and extending the progress achieved in the first phase,
while also strengthening and intensifying the reform process in selected
areas where needed. It would accordingly be the successor to the First
Education Sector Credit that was anticipated when the latter was proposed
in the mid-1980s, and as a continuation of it, would be broadly similar in
structure, rationale, objectives, and type of lending support. The second
project would also be one of the Governmentts principal instruments for
working toward the Devpol aims for education, which are reflected in the
revised Ten-Year Education Plan for 1985186 - 1994/95.
4.03      The project would provide financial assistance for specific
investments constituting a time slice of a ten-year expenditure plan for
the sector. These funds would be released as successive steps of the
reform process are achieved. There would be no fast-disbursing element --
no Credit funds would be disbursed against an import list or otherwise
unrelated to specific expenditure within the sector. Thus, the Credit
would be a sector investment project, rooted in specified components, with
a strongly policy-based orientation and content, but no untied financing of
general imports.
4.04      The relationship of this project to the broader expenditure plan
in summarized in Table 4.1, for the time slice covered by the proposed
project period, 1990-95. The total investment required for this period
under the revised plan adopted by Government in consultation with IDA is
MK223.06 million (US$84.17 million in current prices). This requirement
would be met from the sources shown in the table. Financing commitments
for the period total NK112.16 million, or 50.3 percent. To complete the
program, an additional MK110.90 million is required. To close the gap, an
IDA credit of MK97.78 million (about US$36.9 million) is proposed. The
Government would provide an additional US$13.12 million out of national
budgetary resources.

4.05     The policy reform content of the project is summarized in the
Letter of Education Development Policy and associated policy matrix in
Annex A. In order to ensure effective and expeditious implementation of
the reform package, the investments to be supported with IDA financing have
been divided into three distinct sub-projects. Key elements of the reform
have been designated as conditions for release of funds (paras 6.02-6.05)
for expenditures associated with each sub-project, as indicated in Table
4.2. Release of specified funds would take account of progress on other
elements of the reform. In addition, the Government would provide from its
own resources an increase in the annual education recurrent budget in real
terms to the levels mutually agreed by the Government and the Association,
so that the education share of the national budget would reach at least 15
percent by the end of the Plan period.
4.06     IDA and the Governrment would jointly monitor the program at
specific intervals: for Credit effectiveness; one year after Credit
effectiveness and again 30 months after Credit effectiveness. These
reviews would have the following objectives: to monitor the implementation
of the program; and to ensure adherence to the program objectives, fund
release conditions and performance targets (paras 6.04, 6.C5 and 6.06).

4.07     There is a clear logic to the composition of the sub-projects.
Items in sub-project A are (a) those of highest priority, such as effecting
quality improvements at the primary level, (b) those which are
prerequisites for activities to be undertaken in the later years of the
Credit; and (c) investments which would require five years to complete.
Most of the civil works, which are the most visible investments have been
placed in sub-project C, as an additional incentive to carrying out
important difficult reforms. The interconnections between the investment
sub-projects and the policy reform conditions for release of funds have
been designed so that once a particular investment activity is begun it can
always be completed. Thus, for example, when a construction sub-component
is initiated after one set of policy conditions have been met, that work
would be fully funded and able to be completed even if some subsequent
policy reforms, pertaining to some other sub-components, were delayed for
any reason. Also, the project has been designed so that substantial
funding would be available from the moment of project effectiveness, so as
to facilitate rapid progress toward implementation of the entire program.
4.08     The formulation and preparation of the project, including the
interlinked investment sub-projects and policy conditions, have been under
the active leadership of the MOEC in consultation with all relevant other
parts of Government. Extensive discussions have been held over much of the
period of the First Sector Credit, and there is strong backing for the
project design. Over the more than two years that the Government with the
advice and help of IDA staff has been developing the project, several
special events have been held to widen and deepen the dialogue --
including, most recently, a major workshop in February 1989 for all
Malawian institutions with leading roles in education.
4.09     The format for the project -- specific investments within the
context of a ten-year expenditure plan and a policy reform program (blt no
fast-disbursing element) -- was chosen because it suited the circumstances
particularly well. A policy focus was clearly needed in view of the issues
and needs the sector faced, as have been outlined previously. Specific
investment requirements, set within the framework of a sound, sustainable
long-term expenditure plan, also were clearly a priority. Disbursement
against general imports, on the other hand, was not appropriate at this
juncture, either from a macro perspective (because the IDA lending program
clearly had a sufficient share of fast disbursing funds in the pipeline),
or from a sector perspective (because the additional complexities connected
with assuring the funds disbursed to the Treasury for general imports would
redound to the benefit of education were not needed to achieve the results
required).
4.10     A summary description of the project components follows: (1)
quality improvement (37.32 of the total project cost) through (a) curricula
reform and development; (b) improvements in the preparation, production,
and distribution of textbooks and other instructional materials; (c)
development and introduction of new methods for more effective use of radio
in primary schools; (d) improvement of the distance education system used
at the secondary level; (e) development and introduction of low cost
approaches for improving science instruction and the library system at the
secondary level; (f) improvement of teacher training; (g) expansion of the
number of qualified primary teachers; (h) reforms in the examination



- 44 -
system; and (') strengthening the school inspection system; (2) access
imp:ovements (25.3Z) through (a) facilitating community self-help upgrading
of school facilities by providing roofing sheets and limited amounts of
school furniture; and (b) planning and Implementing reforms in promotion
criteria and facilities utilization (double-shifts at the secondary level
and day students at the university); and (c) expansion of the university's
capacity to produce acccuntants, a skill in& extremely short supply; (3)
manasement. budgeting, and planning (30.42) through (a) various
institutional strengthening and capacity building measures focused on the
Ministry of Education and the Malawi Institute of Education; and (b) a
decentralization program for the Ministry; and (4) Improved resource
mobilization and allocation (7.0Z) through (a) reforms in fee structures at
all levels; (b) changes in i:eporting requirements for recurrent and
investment expenditure plans; and (c) increases in the sector's budget
share.
B. Detailed Description
4.11     Paragraphs 4.12 to 4.16 provide a more detailed description of the
project by level (i.e. primary, secondary, post-secondary, education
management and research).
4.12     Primary Leve... (a) Curriculum Revision. Material Development and
In-Service Training: The Credit would strengthen the MIE through technical
assistance for specialists in reading (2 staff years) and evaluation (2
staff years), and with additional local staff (3 positions), provision of 6
low cost staff houses and two vehicles.  (b) Teaching/Learning Materials:
The Credit would support the development, production and distribution of a
new series of teachers guides and textbooks for the first four standards of
the primary school system in English, Chichewa and Arithmetic and General
Studies for standards 2-4. This would be achieved through the enhancement
of existing publishing facilities at the MIE by appointing for 4 years each
a Publishing Manager to oversee the entire process and an editor with
Desktop Publishing (DTP) expertise. Additional equipment would Include 2
PC's plus one AT computer equipped with DTP facilities. The Credit would
also fund 3,000 sets of low-cost locally-made simple teaching sids for use
in standard 1 classes. (c) Teacher Trainings The Credit would fund the
in-service training of approximately 4,500 teachers using the special
teacher training program being developed under Credit 1767-MAI. The Credit
would provide one staff year of expert assistance in educational technology
and two staff years of short-term fellowships for college tutors, and would
fund the running costs of the program including the salary of the project
Director. The Credit would fund all of the costs of the salaries for the
trainees in the first year, and thereafter a part of trainee salaries with
the MOEC absorbing trainee salaries on a sliding scale (para 3.12). MOEC
will assess performance of the trainees carefully, and retain in the
teaching force only those who perform satisfactorily. In addition, Credit
funds would be used to expand an existing teacher training college
(Montfort College) to increase capacity by 360 student places. (d)
Improving the School Broadcasting Systems The Credit would provide funds
to improve the radio system and use it as a tool for improving the quality
of primary education. New programs would be developed which would not be
just supplementary as at present but would be an integral part of teaching
the new curriculum. The Credit would fund the cost of a modest expansion



- 45 -
in broadcasting facilities at Malawi Broadcasting Corporation (MBC), 5,000
solar powered radios, approximately 12 staff years of technical assistance
to develop the eystem and train Malawian counterparts, and the running
costs of the program with Government absorbing the cost on a sliding scale
(Annex K for details). This component would be evaluated yearly.
Conditions of disbursement would be that: (i) the contractor should be
acceptable to IDA; (ii) a pilot phase should demonstrate clearly the
effectiveness of the programs being developed; and (iii) a satisfactory
maintenance and replacement system for the radios and equipment be put in
place. (e) Improvement in the Measurement of Achievement at the Primary
Levels the Credit would fund a testing and measurement specialist for two
years to assiet in developing a national assessment test and to train
Malawian counterparts and would provide funds for upgrading the computer
system and for the administration of the assessment test. During
negotiation, assurance was received that local staff would be available to
work with the consultant (para 6.06 (b)). (f) Primary School Supervision:
The Credit would support the inspectorate by providing the recurrent costs
of 30 additional inspectors and by providing 60 motorcycles. The
Government would take over the funding of the salaries of the inspectors
gradually beginning in the third year. In addition, the Government would
arrange for servicing of the vehicles to be undertaken by the District
vehicle maintenance depots. (g) Roofing Sheets: The Credit would provide
roofing sheets for 228 unroofed classroom blocks and 406 teachers' houses
in rural areas (para 2.20 and Annex G); and (h) Furniture and Eauipment:
Low cost chairs and desks would be provided for 1,000 standard 5-8
classrooms and 2,000 standard 1-4 classrooms (para 2.34 and Annex F). MOEC
would use local craftsmen managed by the Small Enterprise Development
Organization (SEDOM) to produce these chairs and desks, which would
contribute to the development of small scale industry in Malawi.
4.13     Secondary Level:  (a)  The Credit would support expanded access to
secondary education through provision of 10 new double stream Malawi
College of Distance Education centers. During negotiation, agreement was
reached on the granting of financial autonomy to MCDE (para 6.02 (b)). (b)
Curriculum Revision and Instructional Materials Provision: The Credit
would support workshops for the revision of the secondary school curriculum
by MANEB; the development, production and distribution of low-cost science
equipment and kits suitable for the Malawian environment including 3 months
of training within Africa for 5 technicians (1.25 staff years of
fellowships); and the restocking of school libraries and a training program
for school librarians. (c) Supervisions Secondary schools are not
adequately supervised because of lack of transport, therefore the Credit
vould fund 6 additional vehicles to be located in the regional education
offices for use in school supervision and by the Faculty of Education when
supervising trainees during teaching practice.
4.14     Post-Secondary Education.  Credit funds would be used to provide
the Faculty of Commerce at the University with 12 staff years of technical
assistance, 15 staff years of overseas fellowships under a twinning plan
with an overseas university, and provision of business computers, software
and peripheral equipment.
4.15     Inetitutional Development.  (a) Strengthening Education
Managements The. Credit would cover the operational costs of workshops to



- 46 -
assist in decentralizing some of the accounting activities of the
Ministry, and would fund in-service training of headmasters and principals
in school management techniques. The Credit would also fund 28 modest
staff houses in rural areas for district scho-1 inspectors and the three
regional coordinators of the special primary teacher training program and
salaries of 21 accounting and staff development personnel for the regional
offices. (b) Strengthening Education Plannings In order to assist in the
implementation of the major recommendations of the Education Services
Review with respect to planning, the Credit would include financing for a
series of short term consultancies (two staff years in all) to assist in
locally organized workshops aimed at improving the quality of the planning
process at the national, district and regional levels and would fund the
salaries for five years of 4 education planners within the Planning section
of the Ministry, including one at a senior level and one who would be
responsible for monitoring and evaluation, in order to bring this function
up to establishment strength. To assist the Statistical Unit within the
Planning section to keep proper statistics and undertake analyses so
important in any planning, the Credit would fund an additional position of
a statistician. Provision would be made for in-service training to diploma
level (3 years) for 10 additional statistical assistants at the University
(30 staff years in all). (c) Strengthening the Project Implementation
Unit: To strengthen the PIU and assist in localization of the PIU
professional staff, the Credit would fund 4 staff years of technical
assistance of an architect and 4 staff years of a quantity surveyor as well
as 10 staff years of fellowships - 5 for external training in architecture
and 5 in quantity surveying (both tenable only in an African university).
Operational expenditures for project administration excluding local
salaries would be included under the proposed Credit. In addition, the
Credit would provide funds for the support of the school maintenance unit
of the PIU. In order to ensure that funds are disbursed rapidly, the
Credit would support the recruitment for 2 years of a well qualified
financial analyst to be based in the Project Implementation Unit to take
care of forward planning of the Ministry as well as all procurement issues.
4.16     Support to the Center for Educational Research (CER). The Credit
would support the work of the new CER by providing 3 staff years of
technical assistance for a full time researcher and 6 staff months of short
term research consultancies and the operating costs of the CER for 3 years.
The Credit would specifically fund the ongoing multi-year research on
primary school quality as well as tracer studies of secondary school
students and new studies on gender differences in school achievement and on
the factors affecting absenteeism with particular attention to the timing
of the school year (para 2.46).
4.17     Summary of Technical Assistance and Fellowships.  Overall, the
Credit provides for 54.5 staff years of technical assistance (Annex Table
8). A large part of this is required for the radio component and for
Accountancy lecturers -- skills that are unavailable or scarce in Malawi.
Malawians will be recruited for the other technical assistance positions
where possible. In addition, the Credit would finance 58.25 staff years of
fellowships of which 30 staff years would be in-country training and 13.25
in Africa. In addition, the 3 workshops planned during the life of the
Credit would provide training for more than 900 education staff. During
negotiation, assurance was received from Government that contracts for



- 47 -
experts would be executed on time and selection of recipients for
fellowships and for staff positions would be carried out expeditiously
(para 6.02 (f)).

equivalent in.luding customs duties And taxes equivalent to US$1.35
million. The estimated costs and local and foreign exchange shares are
given by component and category of expenditure in Tables 4.3 and 4.4 and
further detailed in Annexes Tables 9, 10, 11, and 12.

4.19       Basis of Cost Estimates.   Construction costs are based on reviews
of recently awarded contracts for similar facilities being built under on-
going IDA projects. Furniture costs for new facilities are based on
existing priced furniture lists and amount to 5 percent of the cost of new
construction. Furniture for existing primary schools, which is to be
purchased from local craftsmen, has been costed on the basis of material,
labor and a management mark-up cost for SEDOM (see Annex F). The cost of
low-cost locally-made roofing for unfinished primary schools and teachers'
houses has been estimated on the basis of information obtained in-country
by the project architect. Equipment and vehicle costs have been calculated
on the basis of existing equipment lists and recent procurements under on-
going projects. Technical assistance costs -- US$80,000 per staffyear of
specialist assistance and US$20,000 for external training -- have been
estimated on the basis of recent prices for comparable technical assistance
in Credit 1767-MAl. Technical assistance costs include housing, travel,
relocation costs, subsistence, office services, fees and recruitment costs.
Overheads are separately costed. The cost of MNK8,000 per student-year for
training at the University of Malawi is based on recurrent unit costs.
Base cost estimates reflect prices as projected for the time of
negotiation.
4.20       Customs Duties and Taxes.  In line with Malawian policies and
practices, items imported especially for the proposed invostment program
would be exempted from customs duties and taxes. Certain items are
expected to be purchased locally on the open market and reimbursement
percentages have been calculated to exclude duties and taxes estimated at
US$1.35 million equivalent on such items as imported building materials
under construction contracts and local purchase of furniture and textbooks.



- 49 -
4.21     Contineencv Allowances.  Physical contingencies of 10 percent have
been added to the base costs of civil works and 5 percent to the base costs
of furniture and equipment. Price contingencies were calculated based on
Bank staff estimates of international and domestic inflation; and have been
applied in accordance with the implementation schedule (Annex Table 13) on
the assumption of the following annual rates of increases
1989    1990    1991    1992    1993    1994      1995
Foreign         5.3      1.3    2.0     2.0     2.0      2.0      2.0
Local          14.7    11.3    5.6      5.0     5.0      5.0      5.0
4.22     Foreign Exchanze Component:  The foreign exchange component,
estimated at US$20.0 million equivalent or 53 percent of the total project
investment cost net of taxes, has been calculated as follows: (a) civil
works and project administration, 65 percent; (b) project administration
and professional services, 60 percent; (c) furniture made by local
craftsmen4 30 percent, and other furniture, 50 percent; (d) locally
produced roofing tiles, 25 percent; (e) equipment and vehicles, 100
percent; (f) textbooks, 75 percent; (g) technical assistance and overseas
fellowships, 100 per_ent; and (h) local training and workshops, 25 percent.
The foreign exchange estimates take into account that the bulk of the
equipment and a considerable part of the building materials would be
imported.
4.23     Recurrent Costs.  Under the proposed project additional recurrent
costs would be generated by (a) recruitment of additional instructors for
the new MCDE centers and the expanded teacher training college, (b)
additional posts in the planning department, inspectorate and regional and
district offices, (c) appointment of 4,500 trainee teachers, (d) the
additional work required during vacations of the staff of the primary
teacher training colleges, (e) operation and maintenance of new buildings,
vehicles and equipment, and (f) incremental budget for the Center for
Educational Research. The total additional recurrent costs generated by
these items would be US$11.9 million over the project life. Consistent
with the Bank's strategy for Malawi as discussed in the Public Expenditure
Review (Report 7281-MAI), the project would finance a substantial portion
of these costs (80 percent). Local salaries, including salaries of trainee
teachers, would be financed on a sliding scale, with the project paying all
of the increment initially, and the Government absorbing these gradually
over three years. At project completion, the additional annual recurrent
costs would amount to 10 percent Lf the 1990/91 MOEC recurrent budget,
however, 80 percent of this is for salaries of the additional 4,500
teachers to be deployed. The long-term recurrent cost implications of the
project are minimized by (a) the very limited capacity expansion through
construction of new faciliti-s and (b) the agreed efficiency enhancing
measures, which would reduce the cost per graduate substantially especially
at the primary level teacher training institutes and at the secondary
level.

total project cost net of taxes, or 100 percent of the foreign exchange
component and 78.6 percent of local recurrent costs net of taxes.
Government would finance 10 percent of project costs.  UNDP is parallel
financing technical assistance complementary to that provided under the
Credit.  No other donors have made commitments.  The financing plan is
summarized in Table 4.5.

- 52 -
(i) Civil works valued at US$ 4.7 million would be procured
through International Competitive Bidding (ICB) in
accordance with Bank Group guidelines. Civil works valued
at $3.2 million would be procured through Local Competitive
Bidding (LCB) since these would consist of a large number of
small value contracts which are scattered all over the
country and are spread over time. The local tendering
procedures for Malawi are generally acceptable. The average
value of each contract is about US$150,000.
(ii)  Goods required for the project include equipment,
vehicles, furniture, roofing sheets and textbooks.
(a)   Equipment and vehicles valued at US$3.3 million would
be procured through ICB in accordance with Bank Group
guidelines. Materials such as paper, pencils and
other instructional material required in small lots
and quantities during the lifetime of the project
valued at US$2.0 million would be most appropriately
procured through prudent shopping with quotations from
a minimum of three bidders. The average value of each
of these lots is expected to be lower than US$5,000.
(b) Furniture valued at US$1.8 million would be procured
through LCB since these are simple items locally
available. SEDOM would coordinate this. Where LCB is
not practical SEDOM would subcontract the requirement
to small village joiners and carpenters at one center
in each district.  The total value of procurement in
this category is not expected to exceed US$50,000.
(c) Roofing tiles valued at US$1.1 million would be
procured through LCB. It is expected that the various
community production centers assisted by UNDP Habitat
and Housing for Humanity and other organisations
(Annex G) would participate.
(d)   Textbooks valued at US$5.5 million would be procured
through ICB in accordance with the Bank guidelines for
procurement (1985).
(iii) Servicess     Technical assistance valued at US$5.2 million
would be recruited under the Bank Guidelines for the Use of
Consultants, 1981. Architectural firms for civil works
would be selected in accordance with Bank Group guidelines.
All fellowships and training workshops financed by the
project would be carried out under terms and conditions
acceptable to IDA.
(iv)  Recurrent costs consisting of local salaries (US$8.3
million) and operating costs (US$3.7 million) would follow
regular Government procedures and would be reimbursed on the
basis of actual expenditures. The operating costs include



- 53 -
fuel, maintenance of vehicles and equipment including data
processing facilities.
4.26     The threshold for prior review by IDA would be US$100,000.  More
than 70 percent of procurement contracts would be subjected to prior
review. The first LCB document would be reviewed by IDA before its release
for bidding.
P. Disbursement
4.27     Credit proceeds would be released in 3 phases. As stated in para
4.05, the project consists of 3 sub-projects. Sub-project A will disburse
over 60 months, sub-project B over 48 months, and sub-project C over 30
months. Disbursement from the Special Account would be against agreed
expenditures.  Disbursement would be on the basis of:
(a)   100 percent of foreign expenditures and 80 percent of local
expenditures for civil works and professional services;
(b)   100 percent of foreign and local expenditures on roofing
materials, furniture, equipment, vehicles and transportation
of furniture and equipment to school sites;
(c) 100 percent of foreign expenditures and 80 percent of local
expenditures on textbooks;
(d)   100 percent of foreign expenditures and 80 percent of local
expenditures for technical assistance, fellowships and
training;
(e)   100 percent of salaries of local staff (excluding staff of
the Project Implementation Unit) for FY90/91, 91192 and
92/93 and thereafter 70 percent; and
(f)   80 percent of operational expenditures for project
administration.
Simplified disbursement procedures on the basis of statements of
expenditures would be allowed for the following items: (i) civil works
contracts of less than US$100,000 equivalent and other contracts of less
than US$50,000 equivalent; and (ii) in-service training costs and
operational expenditures of the PIU and the Planning Section of the
Ministry.
4.28     Special Account.  A special account of the equivalent of the
amount US$2.0 million covering 6 months expenditures would be kept in a
commercial bank account on terms and conditions acceptable to IDA, to meet
eligible expenditures for goods and services. Replenishment would be made
up to the amount released on satisfaction of the conditions set out in
paras 6.06(c). Replenishment applications would be accompanied by monthly
statements from the commercial bank, suitably cross referenced by the PIU
to the related application. Amounts to be replenished would take into
account the limits imposed on each package (see paras 6.03, 6.04 and 6.05).



- 54.-
G. Imilementation
4.29     The implementation of the reform program is to be carried out by
the Planning Section of MOEC through the Committee on Monitoring and
Evaluation set up under Credit 1767-MAI. This Committee which is chaired
by the Principal Secretary, MOEC, would be responsible for ensuring that
the objectives of the program are met. The Committee is to provide support
to the Planning Section in the following areas: (a) coordination among
various sections of MOEC; (b) preparation of the relevant reports for the
annual review of progress with the Bank Group; and (c) monitoring and
reporting on progress made in implementing various policies. In addition,
the Committee would supervise the MOEC's budget requests (development and
recurrent budgets) for consistency with programmed activities, staff and
policy action objectives. The performance indicators (Annex Table 7) were
agreed upon during negotiation (para 6.02 (h)). In order to help ensure
that the reform program is implemented smoothly and on schedule, the
Government is currently taking steps to strengthen the planning function of
the Ministry so that it would be in a position to implement the reform. In
addition, the responsibility for the reform would be directly with the
Principal Secretary of the Ministry of Education. In order to ensure that
funds are disbursed rapidly, the Credit is supporting the recruitment of a
well qualified financial analyst to be based in the Project Implementation
Unit (PIU) to take care of forward budget of the Ministry as well as all
procurement issues. The PIU will be responsible for the physical
implementation of the project. The PIU is also being strengthened with two
expatriate architects and a quantity surveyor. The Statistical Unit of the
Ministry would be strengthened with equipment and personnel. A senior
Education Planner would be recruited to assist the MOEC in its education
planning. In addition, the Government assured IDA during negotiation that
adequate funds would be made to the MOEC to carry out the investment
program and that no changes would be made to the agreed five-year program
without reference to IDA (paras 6.02(g), 6.03(a), 6.04(b) and 6.05(d)).
4.30     Project implementation is expected to take five years beginning
April 1990. The Project would be completed by December 31, 1995 and will
close by June 30, 1996. The implewentation schedule is given in Annex
Table 13 (for physical implementation) and Annex I for policy programs.
The implementation of the items in sub-project A will be phased over the
five-year life of the project, those in sub-project B over the last four
years of the project, and those in sub-project C over the final two-and-a-
half years of the project.
4.31     Status of Credit Preparation.  An identification mission took
place in March 1988 and a preparation mission in October 1988 at which time
the different elements of the reform package were discussed and agreed upon
with the Government. A workshop on education reform was held in February
1989 to build consensus and ensure MOEC's sense of ownership of the reform
program. An appraisal mission in February/March 1989 reached final
agreement with Government on the reforms and Credit components. Work has
started on a census of all unfinished primary schools, on preparing the
implementation plan for decentralization of the functions of the Ministry
of Education and on estimating the required development and recurrent
budgets for Education for the rest of the Plan period. Low cost furniture
for schools has been designed and costed. In addition, the PIU has



- 55 -
detailed architectural drawing of all the buildings to be financed under
the Credit.
4.32     Impact on the Environment.  The Credit finances school Inputs
primarily and has no impact on the environment.
4.33     Impact on Female Education.  The Government has a policy of open
access to primary education. The policy reforms which are integral to this
project would have substantial positive impact on female enrollment at the
primary level. The gross enrollment rate for girls at this level is
expected to increase from 54 percent in 1986/87 to 70 percent by 1994195
(para 3.36). Since a third of all teacher training places are reserved for
girls, the planned increase in the number of primary school teachers would
provide employment to about 2,000 female teachers.
H. Reportint and Monitorinx
4.34     Auditinz. Monitoring and Reporting.  All project accounts
including the special account would be audited annually by an independent
auditor satisfactory to IDA. The audit statement would include a separate
paragraph with an opinion with respect to amounts withdrawn on the basis of
statements of expenditure. Continuous monitoring of the progress of the
reforms would be the responsibility of the strengthened planning section of
the MOEC while day to day responsibility for ensuring that available Credit
funds are disbursed in time would be the responsibility of the PIU. At
each designated review period, there would be a review of the achievement
of the reform objectives by the Government and IDA, in order to ensure that
conditions for funds release are met. At the same time, the MOEC forward
budgets would be reviewed. The Government would submit to IDA semi-annual
progress reports and within six months of Credit closing, it would submit a
report on project implementation and achievements.



- 56 -
V. BENEFITS AND RISKS
5.01      Benefits.  By supporting the Government program for educational
reform, the Credit would have short and long term benefits contributing to
higher quality education, greater efficiency and equity, and a more cost
effective system. In the short run, primary school pupils would have
improved access to textbooks. The proportion and absolute number of
trained teachers in primary schools would increase significantly and the
frequency and quality of school supervision would improve. More relevant
curricula, a more effective examination system and better learnirg
materials in primary and secondary schools would be introduced. There
would be expanded access (a) at the primary level, directly benefitting
children who would otherwise receive little or no education, and (b) at the
secondary level through the introduction of the shift system. The
additionai MCDE centers would increase the chances of getting a secondary
education. These improvements in access would be especially beneficial for
rural areas and poor families. In the medium and long run the Credit would
improve internal efficiency by reducing repetition and dropouts thus
lowering the cost per graduate. By making the system more cost effective,
the program would free resources for quality improvements at this level.
The quality of instruction in mathematics and English would be improved in
the lower primary levels through innovative use of radio instruction. The
program would assist Government in meeting the demand for middle and high
level managers by strengthening post-secondary commerce and business
programs. Cost recovery at post primary levels would increase, freeing
resources for further expanding and improving the system. The wide ranging
reforms require strong and efficient sector management and a major benefit
accruing from this operation would be the expansion and strengthening of:
(i) the planning function including its data gathering and analytic
capacity, and (ii) educational research capabilities. The PIU would be
improved. Improved methods of assessing children's achievement would be
introduced. Local craftsmen would benefit considerably as the suppliers of
roofing and school furniture.
5.02 Risks. There are several potential risks involved in successfully
implementing this Credit and the policy reforms on which it is based.
First, Malawi's present difficult fiscal situation constrains Government
expenditure, and poses the risk that education's development and recurrent
budget allocations may not increase enough to allow timely implementation
of the investment plan and adequate funding of the expanded system, and
that cost savings and increased cost recovery from the education system,
instead of being recycled to improve educational quality, might be diverted
to other activities of the Government. This risk is minimised by
Government's commitment (stated in Devpol) to increasing education's
budgetary share, and by the requirement that the Ministry of Education
submit its forward budget estimates for the phase of the education plan
that is supported by the Credit for approval by the Finance Ministry before
Credit effectiveness.  Second, there is a risk that the radios to be
provided under the Credit might not be properly maintained.  This risk is
minimised by the requirement that the Government simultaneously put in
place a maintenance system which can be continued even after the Credit
Account has been closed. Third, unless skillfully presented to the public,
there might be strong opposition to increased cost recovery at post primary



- 57 -
levels even though a loan scheme is already in place at the tertiary level.
This risk is mitigated by the fact that Bank staff and Government officials
have spent a considerable amount of time discussing in detail all the
activities to be undertaken during this period in order to build a
consensus. A high level of Bank supervision is planned and the regular
public expenditure and project reviews will also help to ensure that the
objectives of the Credit are met. Fourth, the ability and willingness of
communities to build new classrooms might not keep pace with the expansion
in primary enrollment. This risk is reduced by the provision of roofing
sheets under the Credit to assist communities, and by close liaison between
MOEC and local development committees (who coordinate self-help efforts).
Furthermore, school building is an obviously suitable activity for the
public works (with in-kind payments) that are being considered by
Government (with IDA assistance through the work on food security). Fifth,
the large number of trainee teachers to be recruited into the new three
year in-service training program may not all perform satisfactorily. This
risk is being addressed through conditionality that requires MOEC to
develop criteria against which to evaluate their academic and practical
performance and to release from service those who are unsatisfactory.



- 58 -
VI. ASSURANCES  AND
6.01      During negotiations, the Government reconfirmed its commitment to
implement the actions outlined in the Letter of Education Development Policy
dated August 28, 1989, and the associated Policy Action Program (Annex A and
the attached policy matrix). In line with the schedule agreed with the Bank,
the principal actions to be carried out include: curricula reform at the
primary, teacher training and secondary levels; restructuring of the Ministry;
revieion of the content and format of the primary school leaving examination,
strengthening of the planning function and the inspectorate, and reduction in
the level of subsidy at the post-primary level.
6.02      Within the context of carrying out this program, the Government has
provided assurances that it will proceed, in accordance with agreed schedules
(Table 4.2 and para 4.05), with the implementation of:
(a)  the plan and recommendations of the Education Services Review;
(b)  the three year plan for 198911991 for quality improvement of the
MCDE (para 4.13);
(c)  the revisions to the scope and structure of the PSLC examination;
(d)  the changes in the membership of the National Council of Education;
(e)  the conversion of the refundable secondary school fee to a non-
refundable fee;
(f)  the creation of all posts to be funded by the Credit (para 4.17);
(g)  the adjustments in the level of the development and recurrent
expenditures for education (para 4.05 and 4.29);
(h)  the utilization of updated performance indicators to monitor and
evaluate improvements in quality and efficiency in schools (para
4.29 and Annex Table 6); and
(i)  the increases in fees at the university level and for boarding at
the secondary level (paras 3.23 and 3.34).
These and other measures in the action program are associated with conditions
for credit effectiveness and for subsequent disbursements of funds as follows.
6.03     The conditions for Credit effectiveness (and for release of funds
for sub-project A as defined in Tables 4.2 and 4.3) are:
(a)  agreement with IDA on development and recurrent budgets for
education for 1990/91-1992/93 and indicative budgets through
1994/95 (paras 4.04 and 4.29).
(b)  MOEC prepare and submit a plan satisfactory to IDA, to evaluate the
performance of trainee teachers at each stage of their three-year
academic and practical training cycle, including criteria and
procedures for dismissing poor performers (para 4.12).



-59 -
6.04      The conditions for release of funds for sub-project B (to be
initiated in July 1991) are:
(a) implementation of (i) the first phase of the decentralization of
the MOEC; (ii) the new rules for admission into standard 1; and
(iii) new rules for repetition;
(b) agreement with IDA on: (i) reduced scope of the secondary school
curriculum; and (ii) level of the Development Budget through
1993/94 and indicative budgets through 1995196 (para 4.29); and
(c)  increase in secondary ochool tuition fees from MK30 to MK45 per
year ard in university fees from MK200 to MR280 per year (para
3.23).
6.05      The conditions for release of funds for sub-project C (to be
initiated in January 1993) are:
(a) implementation of the agreed second phase of the Education Services
Review, including the staff development program (para 6.02(a);
(b)  increase in secondary school tuition fees to MK60 per year and
university fees to MK360 (para 3.21);
(c)  increase in secondary school boarding fees from MR150 to MK200;
(d) agreement with IDA on the level of the Education Development Budget
through 1995/96 and indicative budgets through 1997198 (para 6.29);
and
(e)  discontinuation of the provision of exercise books and writing
materials free of charge to secondary school qtudents.
6.06      In addition, Government has agreed that:
(a)  changes in the education investment program for 1991-94 would be
made only in agreement with the dank (para 3.15);
(b)  all posts supported by the project would be filled (and all local
counterparts for consultants would be named) according to the
timetable discussed during negotiations (para 4.12); and
(c) the agreed procedures for the utilization of a special aceount
would be followed )para 4.28).
6.07      Subject to the above assurances and conditions, the proposed
project would constitute a suitable basis for an IDA Credit in the amount of
US$36.9 million equivalent on standard terms to the Government of the Republic
of Malawi.
