Dear Mr. Jaycox,
LETTER OF EDUCATION DEVELOPMENT POLICY
I am writing to you in connection with the proposed Second
Education Sector Credit to inform the International Development
Association of the steps the Government has already taken or is
planning to take to improve the educatlonal system in Malawi.
Through a process of restructuring, reforming and expanding the
education system, the Malawi Government aims at developing a more
effective system with increased access at all levels that is
sustainable,  given  available  resources.       The  Government's
development objectives are elaborated in the Ten Year Education
Plan 1985/86 to 1994/95 and were communicated to IDA. The
recently published Statement of Development Policy reaffirms our
policies and goals.
As you are aware, the Government commissioned the firm of
Price Waterbouse to carry out a thorough review of our Education
services.   The report has now been received and  its major
recommendations accepted. The Government is fully aware that the
long term economic and social development of Malavi is heavily
dependent on the educational level of its population, and it is
also conscious of the fact that the social sectors, particularly



- 61 -
ANNEX A
Page 2 of 6
education,  have been underfunded  in the past.   The serious
economic constraint which Malawi now faces makes it essential to
use available resources in the most efficient and effective way
possible, and to supplement the Government budget with additional
resources.
Achievements in Education
Despite the severe resource constraint, the Government has
continued to expand access to primary and secondary education and
to improve efficiency and quality. The most important measures
taken are as follows:-
(a)   Primary Level:  (i) the primary school curriculum is
being revised so as to make it consistent with our
socio-cultural environment; (ii) steps bave been taken
to greatly reduce the incidence of repetition; and
(iii) a multi-year research project on factors
affecting the quality of primary education has just
been completed.  Report is being finalized.  Initial
results have assisted in our policy formulation.
(b)   Secondary  Level:  (i)  boarding  fees  at  secondary
schools now cover food costs;  (ii) MCDE tuition fees
now cover 30S of recurrent cost.   A link bas been
establihebd  between  the  International  Extension
College of the UK and MCDE to strengthen management
and Improve quality.  Steps are underway to make MCD';
a  self-accounting  institution  with  a  measure  of
autonomy.
(c)   Teachint Training   the one yetz upgrading programe
for untrained teacbers is in place.   This programme
has recently been evaluated and steps have been taken
to remedy the deficiencies.



- 62 -
ANNEX A
Page 3 ol 6
(d)   University of Malawi: allowance to University students
have been abolished; as of Academic year 1988/89,
students pay net tuition fees of K200. A loan scheme
is in place to assist poorer students.
(e)   Examinations:  the two examination systems have been
merged   to   form   a  National   Examination   Board
responsible for preparation and administration of all
examinations.
Proposed Reform 1989 - 1995
Over the period 1989/90 - 1994/1995, the Government is
planning to institute a major reform of the education system.
The objectives and timetable are set out in the attached policy
matrix. The major elements are as follows:-
1.    Primary Level:   Adjustments will be made within the
education  budget  to  ensure  that  the  available
resources are used to improve the quality of education
and expand access especially at the primary level.
(i) Registration of all primary school pupils will
begin in October, 1989. By July 1990 registration of
all primary school pupils will be completed.   The
system will facilitate the monitoring of pupils'
progress and help us control repetition.   (ii) A new
pupils' assessment procedure will be initiated to
minimize the incidence of repetition at the lower
levels.     (iii)  Revision  of  the  primary  school
curriculum  will  be  completed.       (iv)  A  complete
revision of primary level text books to bring them in
line with the new curriculum will be undertaken. (v)
In order to increase access, Government will consider
to abolish tuition fees for standards 1 to 4. Parents
of all primary school pupils will continue purchasing



- 63 -
ANNEX A
Page 4 of 6
some writing materials. The Government will assist in
the provision of school facilities by providing
roofing sheets. (vi) A revision of the PSLE will be
completed  by  July,   1990.       (viii)     We  will
substantially improve our schools broadcasting system
by developing new programmes which will be fully
intergrated with the new curriculum.  Radios will be
distributed to all schools when a maintenance system
has been established.
2.    Secondary Level:   Parts of the curriculum will be
revised and the examinations adjusted.  We intend to
produce low-cost teaching aids for secondary schools
using locally available materials. All students will
have access to the required textbooks.   The current
book deposit has been converted to a non-refundable
book rental fee to be retained by each school in a
revolving fund and used to replace worn out textbooks.
Scholarships will continue being offered to students
who are unable to pay school fees.  Local Education
Authorities will be urged to meet the cost of these
scholarships. We are continuing efforts to make more
efficient use of staff and facilities, by converting
some urban schools to double-shifts and by increasing
the student/teacher ratio from 20:1 to 25:1. We will
continue to encourage the establishment to private
schools.   We will also require more accountability
from those schools in terms of maintaining adequate
standards.
3.    Higher Education: Conscious of the financial burden of
education on the national budget, we intend to move
towards   privatizing   the   catering   and   lodging
facilities at the University.  Given the difficulty we
have had in retaining tutors at MCA and the Faculty of
Commerce, we intend to introduce measures to enable



- 64 -
ANNEX A
Page 5 of 6
staff to earn extra income teaching self funding
courses, to reduce substantially the gap between their
earnings and private sector wages for accountants.
MCA fees will be increased to minimize the Government
subvention.   The University will develop a plan to
increase access to several courses using distance
education. The plan will provide detailed costs and a
time schedule.
4.    Teacher Training:   We will  complete out  one year
programme  designed  to upgrade  all  our unqualified
teachers. In addition, we will initiate a once-only
programme to train about 4, 500 new teachers. (JC and
MCE) leavers will be eligible for this special three
year programme which will combine summer residential
coutae in TTCs witb distance education, seminars in
the field and school-based supervision.
5.    Restructuring of the Education Services:  In line with
the recommendations of the Price Waterhouse report,
the Government intends to streamline the Ministry of
Education and Culture by abolishing redundant posts
and decentralizing many of the functions now carried
out at headquarters. This will involve strengthening
the regional offices and devolving many of the school
administrative and personnel matters to the regions.
In order to carry out this major decentralization, a
functional review of the Ministry has been carried out
by the Management Services Unit of DPMT. We have now
in place a plan for implementing the new structure.
Financial implications are being worked out. We will
reorganize and strengthen the planning function within
the Ministry by appointing additional planners to
improve planning within the primary and secondary
sections, culture, higher education and parastatals.
We have prepared a year by year demand driven plan



- 65 -
ANNEX A
Page 6 of 6
which itemize quantitative targets, prioritizes policy
changes,  and  estimates  both  the  recurrent  and
development costs of all aspects of education.   The
Development Plan for 1989-1995 is attached, and will
be subject to annual review, evaluation and updating.
I hope that these details will help to clarify the main
directions of our education effort.   We will, of course, be
willing to provide you with any further information you may
require.
Yours Sincerely,
L.J.Cbimango, M.P.

STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
SECOND EDUCATION SECTOR CREDIT
REPUBLIC OF MALAWI
A PROPOSED SPECIAL PRIMARY TEACHERS'
TRAINING PROGRAMHE FOR MALAWI
PROBLEM
The growing social demand for primary education, reflected by
the substantial increase (14 percent) in enrolment between 1984185 and
1986/87 together with the Government objectives of phasing out untrained
primary school teachers and reducing the pupilsteacher ratio from 63:1 in
1986/87 to 50:1 by 1994/95 will substantially increase the demand for
trained teachers. Primary school enrolment growth rates have increased
from an average three percent between 1979/80 and 1984/85 to about six
percent between 1985/86 and 1987/88. There is potential for enrolment
growth rates to increase more because: (a) less tian 40 percent of the
primary school age cohort (6-13 years) is enrolled in primary schools, (b)
the Government plans to abolish school fees for Grades 1-4, (c) new
policies are being implemented to reduce dropout rates, and (d) the
Government will maintain its policy of open primary education. It is
likely that by 1993 over 1.4 million pupils will be enrolled in primary
schools and there will be a shortfall of over 7,000 trained teachers. If
the Government modifies its projected pupil:teacher ratio from 50sl to
60:1, there will still be a shortfall of about 4,000 trained teachers.
In order to increase the supply of trained teachers, the
Government may either (i) build new colleges at a cost of approximately
MK 30,000 per student place and an average annual recurrent cost per
trainee of about MK 1,800 or (ii) introduce a program based principally on
distance teacher-training which is less costly and which, given adequate
inputs, can achieve results comparable to those of the two-year pre-service
training program. Given the severe financial constraints and the pressure
on Government to restrict public spending, the latter seems more practical.
PROPOSED ORGANIZATION FOR A SPECIAL PRIMARY TEACHER EDUCATION PROGRAMME
Objective: To increase, by about 4,500, the supply of appropriately
qualified primary school teachers in a teacher training scheme.
The proposal is for a three-year primary teacher training
program which combines short-residential courses in the teachers' training
colleges with distance education, seminars in the field and school-based
supervision. The total programme would approximate to the 1,700 hours of
training now provided in the two-year pre-service teacher training
programme. The teacher training syllabi would be revised to bring them in
line with the revised primary school curriculum and accordingly, the scope
and the sequence of the programme would be prepared. All teachers'
colleges would administer the revised syllabi.
For this special teacher training program, learning modules
would be prepared on the basis of revised syllabi. Each module would: (i)
have clearly defined specific objectives; (ii) comprise content and
exercises and (iii) be supported by handouts and taped lectures. Micro
teaching techniques would be utilized during the residential courses and,



-77-                             ANNEX B
Page 2 of 15
where possible, in district-based seminars. Trainees would be required to
purchase their own textbooks. During the training period, each trainee
would prepare at least one main assignment in each core subject area and
two projects per academic year - one comprising action research on a
classroom problem and one comprising subject-oriented research. It is
expected that the teacher training method would be activity oriented and
that trainees would be encouraged to utilize the school environment, to the
extent possible, as a source of materials for preparing teaching aids. Ten
percent of the overall final grade for trainees will be allocated to
performance in this aspect.
Trainee Selection and Certification
Entry requirement for the special training programme would be
similar to the pre-service programme. However, priority would be given to
candidates who hold the MSCE certificate and those who have failed the
examination but have completed Form IV. On successful completion of the
teacher training programme, trainees would be awarded the T2 or T3
certificate similar to their counterpa_ts in the pre-service programme.
Orfanization of the Programme
The special training programme would offer about 1,700 hours of
training - 840 hours of residential training, 144 hours of non-residential
workshops and the equivalent of 716 hou::s of assignments and supervised
teaching (Table 1.1). At the teachers' colleges, trainees would receive
formal lectures. The college tutors or other suitably qualified persons
would conduct the training programme and subsequently would be responsible
for preparing and marking trainee assignments during the term. Trainees
would be given eight weeks of orientation intended to give them some
pedagogical skills before they are assigned to schools. In the schools,
trainees would be supervised by inspectors and head teachers. During the
school year, about ten days of training workshops would be held by school
inspectors at district level. These workshops would focus on strengthening
common weaknesses identified among trainees in a district and allow for the
exchange of classroom experiences among them. In addition, the teaching of
such areas as art and craft physical education and music would be
undertaken in these workshops in order to ensure that they are related to
the local environment.
M14natement and Administration of the ProEramme
In order to gain experience in the preparation and
implemettation of the programme, a team of about five members drawn from
MOEC, MIE, Faculty of Education and TTCs should undertake a study tour of
two countries which have implemented similar teacher training schemes.
The Programme would be managed by a Coordinating and Monitoring
Committee comprising representatives from the inspectorate, teachers'
training colleges, Teacher Training Unit, registrar (MOE primary section)
Malawi Institute of Education, Faculty of Education, MANEB and regional
education offices (Appendix 1). A coordinator, under the direction of the
Committee, would be responsible for the day-to-day management and
administration of the Programme. He/she would be assisted by three
regional trainang officers (one for each region) and a small support staff.
The coordinator would be secretary to the Committee (Figure 1.1).

ORGANIZATIONAL STRUTURE

Head Teachers             Head Teachers           Head Teachers
|Teacher Trainees|        |Teacher Trainees         Teacher Trainees
All trainees would be registered as external students of specific colleges.
Trainees would be employed as temporary teachers and be remunerated
accordingly. In order to avoid problems of staff housing, efforts would be
made to assign trainees to schools in proximity to their homes. However,
if housing is available elsewhere, trainees placement away from home would
be at the discretion of the district officer.



-80-                          ANNEX B
Page 5 of 15
At the end of the training programmes, those principals and
inspectors who performed outstandingly well in their districts would be
given awards comprising medals and current books on school administration
and supervision.
Approximately twelve months would be required for planning and
preparing the programme and for training 3,008 personnel who, to various
extents, would be involved in its administration and management. The
categories of persons to be trained would comprise district inspectors of
schools (DISs) including the home economics officers (102), district
education officers (DEOs) (56), college tutors (200) and headteachers
(2,650). Eight technicians would be trained to maintain the audio-visual
equipment in the teachers' colleges. Script-writers would also be trained
to undertake the preparation of the training modules.
MOE will enter into a formal contractual arrangement with MIE to
undertake the training of all DEOs and DISs. Subsequent to the development
of the programme, MIE would be expected to:
(i) provide detailed training syllabi, training plan and budget
identifying categories of expenditure (e.g., transportation,
board and lodging, teaching materials and staff costs);
(<i) collaborate with MOE to compile the list of officers for
training;
(iii) prepare and present to the Coordinating and Monitoring Committee
a comprehensive report on each training workshop.
The DISs, in turn, would train primary school headteachers in batches of
about 26 at the district level. The role and function of each category of
personnel are detailed in Appendix 2.
Implementation Schedule
The schedule of activities is summarized in Appendix 3.
Civil Works
In order to provide office space for the coordinator, repairs
would be undertaken to one building at the Lilongwe TTC. All colleges
would be provided with adequate storage facilities for the audio-visual
equipment.
Additional Personnel, Equipment and Vehicles
The core staff, equipment and vehicles to be provided under the
project would be as follow:s

Technical Assistance and Local Trainins
Three staff-years of technical assistance would be provided -
one staff-year of expert assistance to train and advise educators who would
be involved with the development of teaching tapes, two staff-years of
short term fellowships for eight college tutors to pursue courses in
educational technology and study tour for five education personnel.
Workshops would be held to familiarise district education officers (DEOs),
district inspectors of schools (DISs), including the home econoamics
lnspectors, with the training programme and what is required of them in rhe
field. College tutors would be trained in special college-based intensive
workshops. There would be a total of 643 days of workshops for all
participating educators. A ten-day workshop would be held for eight audio-
visual technicians, one from each teachers' college, to train them in the
care and maintenance of the new equipment.



-82 -                              ANNEX B
Page 7 of 15
COST AND FINANCING
The total initial investment cost comprising equipment and
development of training modules is estimated at about MK 4.42 mllion.
Approximately MK 1.7 million would be financed under the First Sector
Credit and the remaining MK 2.7 million would be provided from the proposed
Credit. The total ogeratint cost over the five-year implementation period
would be about MK 16.9 million. Salary payment to trainees would comprise
about 80 per cent (NK 13.2 million) of the total recurrent cost.  The per
trainee cost excluding salary payments, is approximately MK 830 compared
with MK 3,600 in the pre-service programme, and about MK 3,755 if salaries
are included. See Appendix 4 for detailed costs and assumptions.
Conclusion
The success of the programme will rest mainly on the:
i)     quality and quantity of course materials;
-i)    experience and commitment of the programme co-ordinator;
iii)   adequacy of pre-planning;
iv)    academic level of the trainee in-take;
v)     adequacy of supervision provided by headmasters and
inspectors; and
vi)    provision of incentives for colleges staff to undertake
the assignment.
Although this training programme is supposed to be undertaken only once,
the training package, once prepared, would be available if needed in
ieture. Among the other benefits of the programme are the following:
(a) The supply of trained teachers will increase without incurring
extensive capital investment.
(b) There will be improved collaboration between the inspectorate
and the TTCs in the training of primary school teachers.
(c)  'rue trained teachers should be better able than their pre-
service counterparts to cope with the realities of the primary
school classroom.
(d)  The teacher training materials prepared for the special teaching
programme can be utilized in the pre-service programme.
(e)  leadteachers would have received training in classroom
supervision.
(f)  School inspectors would be better prepared to advise teachers on
appropriate pedagogical techniques.



- 83 -                             ANNEX B
Page 8 of 15
APPENDIX 1
CO-ORDINATING AND NONITORING COMITTEE
The special teacher educatiin programme will require
collaboration among the many units and departments involved in its
implementation. Recognizing that a breakdown in communication or diversion
from the focus of the programme would disrupt its progress, a Co-ordinating
and Monitoring Committee comprising representatives from the participating
agencies will be established.
The Co-ordinating and Monitoring Committee will comprise
representatives from:
Ministry of Education - Inspectorate
- Teacher Training Unit
(including the programme co-ordinator)
Faculty of Education   - University of Malawi
Malawi Institute of Education
-lalawi National Examination Board
Teachers' Training Colleges
The Committee would have a maximum of ten members and its secretary would
be the programme co-ordinator.
FUNCTIONS:
1.        Analyse the annual programme plan and budget for the special
teacher education programme to ensure that they are in line with the
objectives of the programme.
2.        Review, on a quarterly basis, the Implementation progress of the
programme identifying hindrances to smooth iplementation and advising on
changes to enhance its effectiveness.
3.        Advise on necessary measures to ensure that each office or
department is conversant with its role and responsibilities in the
implementation of the programme.
4.        Commission an annual evaluation exercise and analyse the
findings with a view to making recommendations for improvement, if
necessary, in the areas of curriculum, organization and management and
operations.

APPENDIX 2
Page 1 of 4
EDUCATIONAL TECHNOLOGY SPECIALIST
Terms of Reference
MALAWI SPECIAL TEACHER EDUCATION PROGRAMNE
Training and re-training of teachers will be an integral part of
the primary education reform programe. The new teaching methologies in
the curriculum must be passed on to teachers with minimum distortion. One
way of ensuring that the teacher trainees in the distance education
programme receive adequate training in the new methods of teaching is to
strengthen the use of educational technology in their training programme.
The audio-visual aids to be utilized In the programme will include video
and audio cassette recordings.
The specialist in educational technology will be required for
one staff-year and will work out of the programme co-ordinator's office,
and in collaboration with MIE and the Faculty of Education tot
i)     identify areas of the teacher training syllabi which can
be effectively supported by video and audio cassette
recordings;

programmes;

audio-visual programmes; and
iv)    prepare a handbook on the use of audio-visual aids in the
training of teachers.

REGIONAL TRAINING OFFICER
Terms of Reference
MALAWI SPECIAL TEACHER EDUCATION PROGRAMME
In keeping with the efforts of MOEC to decentralize its
operations with a view to increasing efficiency in the field, three
training officers will be appointed, one for each region, during the
implementation of the teacher training programme.
Each regional training officer will be expected to:
i) undertake administrative tasks associated with the distance
teacher training programme (includiu: payment of allowances,
organization of workshops and production and distribution of
training materials and purchase of stationery etc.);
-i) in consultation with the DISs and DEOs, develop a schedule for
supervision of trainees and monitor its Implementation;
iii) maintain liaison with the programme co-ordinator, the district
offices and the colleges to ensure that training materials are
distributed on time and that assignments are marked and returned
to trainees promptly;
iv) be alert to hindrances to the implementation of the Programme
and to work within his sphere of authority to remove them or
refer them to the co-ordinator of the programme; and
v)   prepare quarterly reports on the implementation progress of the
training programme in the region giving (a) statistical data --
number of trainees, dropout, attendance at training workshops
and other relevant data, (b) performance rating, (c) brief
description and analysis of problems faced in the region, and
(d) recommendations for improving the Implementation of the
training programme.
Qualifications:
The regional co-ordinator should have:
i)   at least a trained teacher certificate and a bachelor's degree;
ii) about three years teaching experience (teaching experience in a
teacher training institution would be an asset); and
iii) some experience in planning and organizing workshops and
seminars.

STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
REPUBLIC OF MALAWI
SECOND EDUCATION SECTOR CREDIT
PLANS FOR CURRICULUM REVISION
Introduction
It has been nearly twenty years since the curriculum in
Malawi has been revised. The MOEC is now in the midst of a
thorough revision of the curriculum and is approaching the
process with seriousness and care. Through a series of
workshops, the MOEC will have completed by the end of April,
1989, the following:
o    a list of national objectives for education,
o    a list of objectives for each subject area in each
standard,
o    a scope and sequence chart for each subject area, and
o    a teaching syllabus for every subject in each standard.
Timetable
The MOEC plans to revise the curriculum according to *the
following schedule:
W - Write materials and carry out intensive formative evaluation
in a few schools
T - Test completed materials in larger samples of schools in all
regions
P - Print and distribute materials to all schools
I - Implement the materials on a national basis
Schedule:
10/89  10/90  10/91  10/92  10/93  10/94  10/95  10/96  10/97 10/98
1W      1T      IP    1I
2W     2T    2P      21
3W    3T     3P       31
4W     4T       4P     4I
5W     5T       5P     5I
6W      6T      6P     AI
7W     7T      7P     71
8W     8T      8P     81



- 92 -
ANNEX C
2 of 13
Materials
For each standard, pupil's books, teacher's guides, and any
other instructional materials will be developed simultaneously.
The instructional materials, including at least teacher's guides
and pupil's books, will be prepared in the following areas.
Subject        Class         1 2  3  4  5  6  7  8
Mathematics                  x  x  x  x  x  x  x  x
Chichewa                    x  x  x  x  x  x  x  x
English                     x  x  x  x  x  x  x  x
General Studies (*)             x  x  x
(*) Agriculture                          x  x  x  x
(*) Social Studies                       x  x  x  x
(t) Science and Health                   x  x  x  x
Home Economics                           x  x  x  x
For English, Chichewa, Mathematics and General Studies
(Standards 1-4), teacher's guides will be printed for every
teacher and pupil's books will be printed for every two students.
Svecial Areas of the Curriculum
Reading is so important that additional resources will be
devoted to strengthening reading skills in all subject areas.
The organization of the curriculum is based on academic
subject areas; within each subject the curriculum documents focus
on the content to be taught.  In order to give the development of
academic skills more prominent attention, the matrix for each
teaching syllabus should be expanded to include skills.  This
will provide the curriculum developers guidance on how to
syst2matically develop skills in the pupil's books and teacher's
guides. More direct attention should also be given to the
formation of attitudes.
Another important area deserving special attention is the
development of appropriate assessment instruments.  Teachers need
diagnostic tests and other means of assessment to help them
determine how each student is progressing and where remedial
attention is needed. In addition, reliable and valid year-end
tests will help teachers make decisions about promotions. Such
tests could also be used at the beginning of the year for helping
teachers develop a teaching plan and to identify individual
students in need of remediation.
As the MOEC encourages teachers not to hold back students,
there will be an even greater need for good diagnostic tests.
Furthermore, teachers may need additional materials, particularly



93 -
ANNEX C
3 of 13
for remediation, to help them cope with a wider range of student
abilities in their classes.
Finally, MIE will give attention to developing teaching aids
based on locally available materials. The curriculum developers
will help to identify where such materials could be used
effectively by teachers. MIE will study what teaching aids can
be prepared from local materials and how to enable and encourage
teachers to develop these aids. Possibly some low cost
instructicnal materials such as charts, for example, coulA be
produced and distributed to teachers as well.
Developing the Materials
Most of the instructional materials will be developed by
small groups of writers to be drawn from the subject committees.
During each writers' workshop the writers will receive training
on how to prepare pupil's books and teacher's guides. Each group
will then allocate different topics to each writer. Much of the
work will be completed at the workshop; in some cases the writing
will extend beyond the workshop. The MIE professional officers
will be responsible for pulling together the various components
and preparing the materials for further review.
The curriculum developers will work with'the radio Producers
to help ensure that the new radio programmes to be produced will
support the new curriculum as effectively as possible.
The process of developing the materials will include a
careful review of the documents at each stage; outside
consultants provided by the UNDP and IDA will assist with this
review.
Between April and September, 1989, the revision of the
materials (course objectives, scope and sequence, and teaching
syllabuses) will begin. The consultants will work with the
subject committees in suggesting revisions. This process will be
completed and revisions made for each standard before writing
begins on the pupil's books and teacher's guides.
Subject to the proviso above, writing will begin on the
Standard 1 subjects in October, 1989, following an orientation
workshop for writers in September.
As the instructional materials are being prepared,
interchange will continue between the writers and consultants.
Another important part of the process of developing the
materials is formative evaluation, an evaluation process that
seeks to improve the materials as they are being created. As the
instructional materials are being developed, they will be tested



94 -                  ANNEX C
4 of 13
in selected schools near MIE. The writers and evaluators will
develop an evaluation program that involves regular visits to the
schocls, periodic testing of student achievement, and interviews
with teachers. The purposes of this evaluation are to (a) revise
the materials being tried out and (b) to help guide or modify the
development of the remaining topics of the syllabus.
A much broader, but less intensive, formative evaluation
will be carried out during year 2 for each standard (the year for
"testing" the materials). A representative sample of schools
will be selected from all three regions of the country. Feedback
will be obtained mostly through questionnaires to be completed by
the teachers.
B.   Resources to be Provided
In order to carry out the curriculum development process
including the special features described above, the Second
Education Sector Credit will provide the following:
1.   Technical assistance (4 staff years)
For terms of reference, see attached sheets.
a.   Reading Specialist (2 staff years)
b.   Evaluator (2 staff years)
2.   Additional MIE staff
Additional MIE staff are needed to develop all instructional
materials simultaneously, to conduct formative evaluations, and
to prepare two years of materials starting in 1991. IDA will
provide funding for three addicional professional officers at MIE
to be drawn from the areas of subject matter specialists,
evaluation, and instructional materials design.
3.   Staff houses
IDA will f"nd the construction of six low-cost staff houses
at MIE.
4.   Vehicles
2 four-wheel drive vehicles
5.   Computer
1 IBM PS/2 Model 30 with a 30 mb disk and Epson LX1000
printer to support the evaluation activities

The existing primary  school curriculum is being  revised as

ievel. It is intended that the organiation and structure of the

rnumeracy and the tendency to rote learn rath&r than to understand

:n line with the expectation of the educational reform programme.

(b) Assist subject panels in preparing curriculum evaluation

MALAWI INSTITUTE OF EDUCATION
Reading Specialist
Terms of Reference
A main objective of the educational reform programme is to
raise the level of literacy among the Malavian population.
Despite the large  investment already made in  upgrading primary
education, howover, achievement in literacy has been frustrated
by poor teaching methodology, sparcity of teaching/learning
materials and high dropout rates in the lower standards of
primary schools. Hence there is dire need to focus attention on
the teaching of reading in Standards I to 4. It is expected that
the average child, who completes Standard  4 should be, at least,
functionally literate.
The reading spectalist  will work in collaboration  vith the
subjoct panels to;
(a) Review teaching syllabuses for Standards 1 to 4 in ordor
to determine the appropriateness of the methodologies suggested
for the teaching of reading skills, -
(b)-Identify, through research, the reading skilis which can
be taught effectively in Standards 1 to 4,
(c) Identify exercisea and instructional materials vhich
will  reinforce    learning  of reading  skills   in  the  lover
standards,
(d) Prepare prototypes of low-cost reading materials,
(e) Propare training materials and hold training vorkshops
for college tutors and M.I.E professional staff (in all subject
areas) to broaden their experiences in the teaching of reading
throu,n  various   subjoct  areas  and  in   identifying reading
difficulties and appropriate romediat on techniques,
(f) Prepare reading readiness programme for Standard 1,
(g) Through resoarch, prepare a graded reading test for
Standards I to 8 to be administered by teachers, enabling them to
monitor the progres of their pupils,
(h) Establish an experimental reading group and monitor the
reading progres of pupils over onc academic year, and
(i) Prepare an evaluation report on the teaching of reading
in the revised currieu!bm.

STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
REPUBLIC OF MALAWI
SECOND EDUCATION SECTOR CREDIT
Implementation of the Textbook Component of the 2nd EducAtion Sector Credit
General
The component builds on the existing publishing and production capability
at Malawi Institute of Education. Additions aro to be made in a) staffing
and b) equipment.
Staffing
A Publishing Manager will be appointed to provide the overall management of
the publication of the new Primary Schools' textbooks.
A Desktop Publishing Editor (DTP) will be appointed to provide quality
control and to operate the Desktop Publishing technology.
Equipment
Two Personal Computers will be supplied for the generation of 'electronic
manuscripts' i.e. typing materials onto disk for the editor.
A Desktop Publishing System including an AT Computer, A4 screen, seanner,
software and 24 pin Dot Matrix (draft) printer.
Procedure
The subject panels will supply final manuscript to the publishing unit.
The text will be keyed and disks supplied to the DTP editor. The DTP editor
with the assistance of his Design colleagues will do the page layout on
screen and submit the draft print-out for approval by the subject panel.
The agreed final version is then sent to the computer-typesetters (Blantyre
P & P or Montfort Press) with the draft print-out marked up with the
illustrations keyed in. Camera Ready Copy (CRC) is prepared. The CRC is
then sent to the printers who should be selected through International
Competitive Bidding. The final print-run should be agreed with the MOEC
authorities. The finished books will be collected by Malawi Book Service;
MIE pay the printers and MBS pay MIS on the basis of publijhed price less
10X. MBS will then deliver books direct to the DEOs; the distribution of
books will be on a pro rata basis according to student enrolment - figures
supplied to MBS by MIEC. The DEOs distribute books on a strict pro rata
basis using the best means at their disposal.  Haewi Institute of
Education retain copyright and complete control of primary educational
publishing in hslawi and employ their surpluses for the further development
of materials across the curriculum.

ANNEX D
MALAWI              rage I of 6
SECOND EDUCATION SECTOR CREDIT
MINISTRY OF EDUCATION AND CULTURE (MOEC)
MANAGEMENT ISSUES
1.   The March 1988 Education Service Review (ESP) focusses on
three somewhat interrelated management issues at MOEC: planning;
management structures and methods; and staff development. This
paper (i} summarizes the issues and the ESR's proposals for
dealing with them and (ii) makes recommendations for addressing
them.
Planning
2.   MOEC's principal planning document, the Ten Year Plan
(through 1995) demonstrates the weakness of the planning process
at the Ministry. First, the plan itself is flawed. Key targets
- enrollment, pupil/teacher ratios, repeaters - are excluded.
Goals are not prioritized, which makes implementation difficult
in a period of limited resources.
3.   More importantly, the Ten Year Plan appears to have been an
end in itself and is not part of the planning-programming-
monitoring-adjustment process that characterizes responsive
management. In 'fact,'the ESR concludes that there is little or
no monitoring, that the plan is not being addres-ed
operationally, and that, given the lack of interim targets, there
would be great difficulty in establishing reasonable monitoring.
4.   Initial assistance to MOEC's planning division will be
provided by a UNDP-funded UNESCO-executed project (number
MLW/88/042/A/01/13, entitled Strengthening Educational Planning
and Management) which will inter alia develop the division's
capabilities to carry out its policy analysis, planning,
budgeting, monitoring and evaluation responsibilities. The
project will also assist the division in designing and
implementing decentralization (please see para. 10-13)
and establish a computerized management information system.
D.   To change planning *rom a once-in-ten-years separate project
to a continuous process requires both more experienced personnel
and a change in management style. Annual five-year plans, or
rolling plans, should be produced. In line with the
decentralization effort, (please see para. 10-13), planning
personnel should be installed-at the regional level as well ag at
MOEC headquarters. To make the plan more responsive, the
National Advisory Council (NAC) should be reconstituted to
include a broader representation, i.e. non-educators and parents.
1



- 105-
AM=t D
Page 2 of 6
6.   To address the needs of education planning over the longer
term, the following schedule of implementation dates is proposed:
a)   by January 1990:
i)   re-constitution of NAC to include non-educators
and parents and to meet quarterly;
ii) establishment of 16 posts: 12 planning officers in
the regions and four planning officers at
headquarters.
iii) completion and subrission to IDA of the first
five-year education plan;
b)   by July 1990:
filling the 16 ptsts defined in para. 6 (a) (ii);
c)   By January 1991:
completion and submission to IDA of the second five-
year plan;
d)   by July 1991:
completion of in-service training of the 16 posts and
of the regional education officers on education
planning and management;
e)   by each subsequent January:
completion and submission to IDA of a revised five-year
education plan.
Because the personnel filling the 16 new planning positions would
not be fully trained until July 1991, it is recognized that the
first two five-year plans will lack the depth gnd thoroughness
which subsequeiAt versions would include. Nevertheless, it is
vital to start the process as soon as possible so that Ministry
personnel can acquire experience with the planning process.
Management Structure and Methods
7.   One of the more serious impediments to progress in meeting
the country's educational needs is the management of MOEC.
8.   The ESR describes the situation in formalized terms as one
of over-centralization, inappropriate structure, and unclear
definitions. Over-centralisation is characterized by the
practice of high-level MOEC officials occupying themselves with
routine and trivial issues, which leaves them, no time to address
policy or other matters of conseguence. This is reflected in:
the Ministry's organization, which has many one-over-one



- 106 -
ANNEX D
Page 3 of 6
relationships, too many layers, and other anomalies; and the lack
of reporting, blurred relationship, and other anomalies; there is
a lack of definition of authority and responsibility fand hence,
performance criteria) of individuals and of objectives of
subgroups within the Ministry.
9.   More important than its formalized analysis, the ESR
recognizes that the organization and structure reflect the
culture of the Ministry, and that effective change can be made
only by changing the culture.
10. The ESR proposes an organization for the Ministry
substantially different from the present one, the principal
feature of which is decentralization: greatly enhanced authority
and responsibility of the three regional offices; and
concentration of headquarters personnel on policy, overall
planning, curriculum development, superv-ision of regional
activities, and research and evaluation. The Government's
Department of Personnel, Management and Training (DPMT) has
drafted an alternative organization which accomplishes
decentralization in the same manner and is satisfactory; this is
attachment I to this paper.
11. While the Government has accepted the ESR recommendations,
it has not yet accepted DPMT's proposed reorganization of MOEC,
nor have definitions of authority and responsibility for any of
MOEC's organizational elements been drafted or approved. While
DPMT may meet its schedule of getting approval of the
organization by March 1989, preparing a plan to implement the
reorganization and defining objectives, authority and
responsibility certainly will not be done by then. As
implementaAion of the project depends on a vastly improved
Ministry management, completion of the reorganization plan,
implementation schedule, and definition of authority and
responsibility of each organizational element within the
Ministry, all acceptable to IDA and approved by all necessary
governmental levels, should be accomplished before negotiations
on the proposed credit begin.
12. While MOEC nominally supports decentralization and has
already transferred some responsibilities to the regional
offices, the ESR suggests that this has largely been lip service;
hence, the urgency of the above conditions for negotiations.
Areas that have not been addressed'on a regional basis are
accounting, administration and personnel/staff development.
While the argument can be (and, indeed, will be) made that these
functions should remain at headquarters because of the
requirement for centralized control, the need for effective
decentralization at the Ministry outweighs the advantages of
centralized control, and effective decentralization requires
devolutio.n of responsibility for finance, administration, and
staff matters to the regional offices. To accomplish this; the
following implementation datep for key support positions are
proposed:
3



- 107 -
AMNEX D
Page 4 of 6
a)  by July 1990, the establishment of the following positions for
the regional offices: twelve accountants, nine staff
development officers, nine administrative officers;
b) By December 199o, filling the above posts.
13. The principal risk of all the elements of reorganization is
that MOEC's culture will not change, in which case the expanded
regional offices will represent an additional (and expensive)
bureaucratic barrier for routine decisions to hurdle on their way
to the top. While cognizance of the risk is necessary, the
alternative to embarking on the reorganization course, viz
pretending that unsatisfactory MOEC management is somehow
improving, would undermine the project's objectives. Putting the
reorganization into effect will create the mechanism and, one
hopes, start the momentum for genuine improvement in the Ministry's
management.
Staff Development
14. The ESR observes aa almost total lack of staff development,
including training, within YOEC, which has a single Training
officer working alone, and n%. other capability in such related
areas as manpower development, skills appraisal, career management
or policy development. This assessment probably is similarly
applicable to most other Ministries, as DPMT has only has only
recently begun to address this area of its responsibility.
15. In the immediate future, the UNDP-UNESCO project (para 4) will
develop MOEC's capability to design and implement an in-service
training program for about 300 staff members in planning and
management.
16. In the intermediate future, a number of DPMT activities which
apply to the entire Government would benefit MOEC. Already
underway, is a USAID-funded project to permit DPMT to upgrade its
personnel-management role, which is now characterized as 70%
effective. This project would put into place a personnel-
management information system for the entire civil service by the
end of cy 1990, with interim goals of survey completion and systems
installation by the end of cy 89. This MIS would permit each
Ministry to undertake a skills assessment to determine the training
needs of its staff. A second activity already underway is an ODA-
funded project to update the schemes of service for Government
employees.
17. Completion of these two elements would enable the recently-



108 -
ANNEX D
Page 5 of 6
established Malawi Institute of Management (MIM) to undertake
training needs assessments of personnel of various Ministries.
Thereafter, it would also offer training to MOW' management and
identify the training needs of the teaching staff to the Malawi
Institute of Education (MIE).
18. To ensure that MOEC's staff development needs are addressed
expeditiously, the Ministry should agree to, in conjunction with
DPMT, develop it own human-resource strategy by July 1991. This
strategy would identify (a) the number of managers, staff and
teachers required over the five-year plan period; (b) the type of
skills required for each job; (c) phasing of staff-development
requirements to meet the personnel targets in (a), taking into
account personnel turnover. This strategy should be updated each
subsequent July.
Summary
19. In addition to establishing a condition of negotiations
(para. 11) and proposing schedules for completion of key steps
(paras 6, 12, and 18), IDA can assist the strengthening of MOEC
management by providing funding for the additional personnel
which are hard to find, i.e. excluding the administrative
officers. Specifically, the proposed credit should provide
funding for four years of service of the following positions:
a)   16 planning officers (para 6);
b)   Twelve accountants (para 12); and
c)   Nine staff-development officers (para 12).
This totals 37 staff or 148 staff-years.
JEG
March 14, 1989

STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT          Page 1 of 8
REPUBLIC OF MUAAWI
SECOND EDUCATION SECTOR CRED!T
INTRODUCTION OF THE SHIFT SYSTEM IN SIX
&ALAVIAN URbAN SECONDARY SCHOOLS
The gross enrolment ratio at the secondary level is only 4.5
per cent of the age-group. Existing socondary schools, organized
as they are on a single shift basis, have provided access for
only  26,000  students.   Because  of this  constraint  in school
places, in 1987, only 11 per cent of tho 66,000 students who
passed the PSLE obtained places in Form I of secondary schools.
Considering that GON accords high priority to equity in the
education system, it is proposed that a sample of urban schools
be organized on a shift basis in order to help meet the demand
for secondary school places in the urban centres.
The shift system would be introduced, on a phased basis,
first, in two schools in LilongSwe,and later in four additional
schools in other urban centres. A total of 200 students would be
accepted in Forms I and III in the first year of implementation.
Form I students would be selected according their performance on
the PSLE and for Form III, students would be select6d from among
NCDE  students  on the  basis of    their performance  on  the JC
exmination. The solection of students for each shift would be
left primarily to the headteachers, while MOEC would provide
broad operational guidelines within which such decisions would be
made.
Equity Consideration
Measures would be taken- by HOEC to ensure that  students on
both shifts are provided similar learning environments with
respect   to   staff   qualification   and   tho   provision   of
teaching/learning  materials.   It may  not be possible to  offer
all subjects on both shifts but this matter will be left to the
discretion of the headteacher.
Staffing
According to the models shown in Appendix 1, about seven
additional, teachers would be required, for each of the six
schools, in the first year of implementing the shift system. The
number of additional teachers. assigned to each school, however,
would be decided on the bsis of full utilization of the existing
staff. Under the shift system, there would be little or no time
for informal interaction between students and their teachers;
hence one period per week would be allocated for Form counselling
and a guidance counsellor's post would be established In each



-Ill -
ANNEX E
Page 2 of 8
school.
Additional support staff woold be provided for each school so
that the staff numbers would follow the general pattern shown in
Appendix 2.
School Administration
Two deputy principals wvald be required - one for each shift.
In a co-educational school, one deputy principal would be a
female. The hoadteacher would work across shifts.
Extra - curricula Activities
Students would naot be deprived of their extra - curricula
activities.    Teachers   on  one  shift  would   supervise  such
activities during the other shift.
Length of the School Day
The school day would begin at 7:00 a.m. and end at 6:30 p.m.
Each shift would last for 5.5 hours, the first starting at 7:00
a.m and the scond at 1:00 p.m.
Physical Requirements
The physical facilities to be provided in each school would
be determined on the basis of individual assessment of the
utilization of tho existing plant. However, there are additional
facilities  such as  staffrooms,  offices, storerooms,  'holding'
areas, teachors houses and student toilets, which schools would
need to various extents (Appendix 3). Additional textbooks,
science materials and equipment and other teaching materials
would be provided.
School Maintenance
The increased pressure on the utilization of the school
facilities will result in rapid deterioration of the school plant
unless an appropriate school maintenance programe is  in place.
HOEC w%uld   employ one  handyman per school  to undertake  minor
maintenance  and, each  school  would  be  given    an   annual
maintenance  grant of about  of  MK15.000  for *"h  purcth-.  c.'
materials needed for the maintenance work. Classroom sanitation
would continue to be the responsibility of students while toilets
would be maintained by the groundsmen.



- 112 -
ANNLX E
Page 3 of 8
Costs
The total capital expenditure is estimated at an average of
MK833,000 per school.When the second shift is fully implemented.

Appendix 2
Staff Requirements for a School
Operating a Shift System
Teaching Staff
The additional teaching staff needs should be determined
on the  basis of  a 25:1  student to  staff ratio.   The  average
teaching load would be thirty 40-minute periods per week.
Support Staff
The support staff for a school on the shift system would
comprise:
1 Executive Officer
3 Clerical Officers
3 Typists
3 Messengers
3 Typists
4 Groundsmen/watchmen
2 Laboratory Technicians



-116.-                      ANNEX E
Page 7 of 8
Appendix 3
Basic Physical Requirements for Upgrading
Schools on Shift
Generally, schools to be organized on a shift basis would be
provlded with following facilities:
X. A multi-purpose holding area of 240m2, roofed, open sided and
a minimum height of three metres, to be utilized an waiting area
for students at the beginning and end of each shift, particularly
during the long rainy season, and for extra-curricula activities,
2. Additional library accommodation of approximately 115m2,
3. Two new standard #ilet blocks of 60m2 for boys and girls,
4. Three T.3 staff houses,
5. Two 12m12 offices for deputy principals,
6.   Increased staffroom accommidation  to allow 3.5m2  per staff
member (also additional shelves and cupboards),
7. Repairs to existing classrooms, laboratories and workshops so
that all teaching areas are functional,
8.    Additional furniture  - .150  double  desks, 15  tables, 100
laborato,y stools, 50 library chairs and 200 stacking chairs,
9.   Additional  school  textbooks,  particularly science  books,
about 1,000 library books and some additional teaching materials.
10. A very high level of janitorial services would be required
because the school plant would be subjected to a much higher use
factor and a speedy cleaning job would be needed between shifts.
Contract cleaning services may be advantageous for schools on a
shift system.
A detailed investigation should be made of the existing
facilities at each school as the extent of the upgrading and the
need for  added facilities will vary  at each school. . The basic
items 1 to 6 above would be added to nearly all existing schools,
in  order   to  ensure   a  satisfactory   environment  for   the
introduction of the shift system.
Some Individual School Requirements
The mission visited five of the six schools to be organized
on a shift basis. On the basis of this preliminary visit, the
following primary needs were identified in the schools:



- 117 -                    ANNEX E
Page 8 of 8
The   Chichiri   Secondary    School   requires    extensive
rehabilitation of the lighting and water systems. The roof of
the administrative block needs to be replaced. The existing
classrooms  and   specialist  areas,   with  minor   repairs  and
furnishing can accommodate the second shift adequately. The other
basic amenities, however, would be needed.
At the Blantyre Secondary School the physical facilities
were in generally  good condition.   The addition of the  primary
facilitiea would be sufficient for the introduction of a shift
system.
The site of the library at the HII Secondary School is
adequate for a double shift school however the furniture and
library stocks are insufficient, even for a tenth of the present
enrolment.
The sewerage system at the Bwaila Secondary School is a
health hazard and  the shift system  should not be introduced  in
this school before an effective sewerage system is in place.
The  Lilongwe  Girls Secondary  School  will require  all the
suggested primary  facilities for a  shift system to  be operated
effectively.

STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
REPUBLIC OF MALAWI
SECOND EDUCATION SECTOR CREDIT
FURNITURE FOR PRIMARY SCHIOOLS
The P.I.U. is presently concluding arrangements with Small
Enterprise Development Organization (SEDOM) for the suppiv of
school furniture.   Design drawings have been prepared for "all
wood" desks and benches.   SEDOM is a parastatal agency partly
funded by donor agencies to develop and finance small manufacturers
and craftsmen.
It is intended that a contract will be entered into with SEDOM
to supply the furniture who will in turn subcontract with small
village joiners and carpenters to produce the furniture at one
centre in each district. SEDOM will have overall responsibility
for quality and delivery to the schools. Tndependent manufacturers
will also be invited to tender for some of the furniture to ensure
competitive pricing during the program. Malawi now has an absundant
supply of. good quality pine and the designers will utilize this
material instead of the more expensive laminated timber in current
use.
By establishing this market we anticipate an enthusiastic
response from the Malawi craftsman to this economic opportunity.

ANNEX G
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
REPUBLIC OF MALAWI
SECOND EDUCATION SECTOR CREDIT
ROOFING FOR PRIMARY SCHOOLS
The roofing material will be the concrete roofing tiles which
are presently made in Malawi. UNDP studies and the mission's own enquiries
have confirmed the suitability of these tiles for roofing the classrooms
and staff house. The concrete roofing tiles are cooler, and have a price
advantage on galvanized sheets. The use of concrete tiles will improve
employment opportunities for local craftsmen and assist in developing a
roofing component which can be expanded in the future to all types of
building construction in Malawi. The technology for the production of
these roofing tiles is quite simple and the techniques of production have
been taught to a number of male and female groups in Malawi. The
constituent materials are sand and cement vibrated on locally made hand-
operated steel vibrating table. The product is therefore entirely locally
=anufactured, with only the cement being Imported and is most appropriate
in view of country's present shortage of foreign exchange. The reservation
expressed is the ability of the local groups to meet the production
targets, to mitigate against this possibility it is recommended that notice
of the quantities required and location of the schools should be given to
the suppliers as early as possible.

PLAN FOR STRNZGTUREING PRIMRY SCUOOL EDUCATION
THROUGH THE USE OF RADIO PROGRAUS
Introduction
The HOEC is committed to using radio programmes t.o strengthen the
quality of education in primary schools. There is over twenty years of
experience in Malawi in the use of schools broadcasting; however, the present
programs are not playing an effective role in the schools for many reasons
including too few broadcasts, an insufficient number of trained staff, lack of
radios in the schools, lack of an appropriate and affordable power supply for
the radios, lack of a maintenance system for the radios, a broadcast schedule
that doesn't fit well the school timetable, and lack of support materials for
the teachers.
With the development of the new primary school curriculum, however,
there is an opportunity to design radio programs that will support the new
instructional objectives. There will be close collaboration between the radio
producers and curriculum developers to ensure that the new radio programs are
integrated into the total instructional package. Further, the Schools
Broadcasting Unit has recommended that the total hours of programming for
primary schools be increased from under three hours per week to fifteen hours.
Within this context, special attention would be given to Standards 1-4
(approximately nine hours) and to the core subject areas.
Note that to make allowance for this amount of broadcast time for
primary schools, the programs for secondary schools and teacher training would
not be broadcast, but would be made available on cassettes.
New Protrs_
The programs to support the new curriculum will build upon the schools
broadcasting experience in Malawi and the existing programs as appropriate.
In addition, the MOEC will examine a variety of approaches to assess which
ones show promise for being effective in Malawi schools.
The proposed radio project will consist of two phases, the second phase
to be dependent on the success of the first phase.
Phase I (1990-91) - Thas would be a pilot phase for one year in which
the capacity to produce the new lessons is developed, and the general approach
or style of the lessons would be worked out. It is recommended that Phase I
focus on one subject in Standard 1 and that a sufficient number of programs be
produced, probably enough for half a year, so that it will be possible to
measure the effectiveness of the lessons.



- 142 -
Poge 2 of 7
An important component of this phase is a summative evaluation1 to
assess the effectiveness of the lessona.   Continuation of the project to
Phase II will depend on evidence that the new programs are indeed effective,
that is, that there are significant gains in achievement as compared to
classes that are not using radio. In addition the MOEC would need to be
satisfied that the lessons are of a type that are appropriate for Malawian
schools and that the long-term recurrent costs of the lessons in terms of
support materials and teacher training are affordable. The MOEC in
collaboration with IDA will make the decision on continuing the project.
Another component of Phase £ is a number of studies that will determine
the selection of the radios, an effective and affordable power supply for the
radios, and a system for maintaining the radios.
Phase II (1991-95) - This phase is for the full implementation of the
project. Staffing, office space, recording studio time, etc. will be needed
to produce seaveral lessons a week in each of approximately three different
subjects. The following schedule is proposed:
July '91 - June '92
Std. 1      produce programs and carry out formative evaluation2
Std. 2      design programs
Test out radios and power supplies
July '92 - June '93
Std.  1     carry out summative evaluation and revise as necessary
Std. 2      produce programs and carry out formative evaluation
Std. 3      design progr-ms
Provide training for inspectors
Continue testing of radio and power supplies
Complete plans for implementing a maintenance system for radios
1 The summative evaluation will measure the impact of the completed
series of radio p'-ogrammes on the learning gains of the children. The
evaluation will compare the achievement gains of children in classes that use
uhe radio programmes (experimental group) with children in classes without the
radio lessons (control group). In this sense, it is a summary' evaluation.
2 In a formative evaluation, the materials are evaluated as they are
being developed. The purpose of the evaluation, which typically includes
regular observations of classes and occasional testing of the children, is to
improve the lessons. The data provide a basis for revising the materials that
have been tried out in the schools, but also for modifying lessons that have
not yet been completed. In this sense, the evaluation helps to 'form' the
curriculum as it is being developed.



- 143 -
AN= l
Page 3 of 7
July '93 - June '94
Std. 1      implement on national basis, first phase
Std. 2      carry out summative evaluation and revise as necessary
Std. 3      produce programs and carry out formative evaluation
Train Standard 1 teachers
Begin implementing radio maintenance system
July '94 - June '95
Std. 1      complete national implementation
Std. 2      implement on national basis
Std. 3      carry out summative evaluation; implement on national basis
Std. 4      produce programs and carry out formative evaluation
Std. 5      design lessons
Train teachers for Std. 1-3
Complete implementation of radio maintenance system
Solicitation of Prouosals
To obtain assistance in carrying out this project, the MOEC will solicit
proposals on an international basis. The proposals will include a technical
proposal and a budget.
Technical Proposal
The technical proposal (30 pages maximum) will describe the offerers
understanding of the project and how the offerer plans to fulfill the project
objectives. In particular, the proposal should includ, the following:
1.    A statement of the offerers understanding of the project.
2.    A description of the types of radio programs to be used including the
style of the lessons, how the programs will support the teachers in teaching
the new curriculum, the number of lessons per week, and the length of each
programme. The proposal should ocate why the particular approach is expected
to be effective given the circumstances in Malawi and based on the experience
sud research on similar approaches in other countries. Suggestions for site
visits should be included.
3.    A description of the process by which the lessons will be produced and
tested.
4.    A description of how the effectiveness of the lessons in Phase I will be
measured, including a plan for a summative evaluation and the criteria to be
used to judge the impact of the programs. Plans for yearly summative
evaluations should be included.
5.    An estimate of the Malawi personnel (number, qualifications and
responsibilities) needed to carry out the activities and how the training of
the personnel will be done.



- 144 -
ANNE& K
Page 4 of 7
6.    The number and time for short-term and long-term consultants that will
be needed, their qualifications, and terms of reference. At least three
individuals should be nominated for each position and CVs included.
7.    A list of equipment and supplies -- office equipment, furniture,
vehicles, studio equipment, and radios and power supplies needed for national
implementation.
8.    A preliminary description of options for radios, power sources, and a
maintenance system for the radios. A description of the studies to be
undertaken to determine final choices.
9.    A year by year timetable for project activities.
10.   A capabilities statement of the institution(s) including previous work
in educational radio and experience in supporting projects in developing
nations.
Budaet
The budget will include the followings
1.    Estimated costs for developing the programs broken down by each phase:
-     local salaries
-     consultants (up to seven staff years) with all benefits, travel
and allowances (GOM will supply housing)
-     travel and per diem (including site visits)
-     equipment for a complete studio
-     office equipment, furniture, vehicles (2)
-     radios and power supplies for national implementation
-     overhead or in-direct costs and fees
2.    Detailed estimated costs for implementing the programs on a national
basis including radios, power supplies, radio maintenance system, print
materials for the students and teachers, cassette tapes (for schools out of
range of the radio station), delivery, and training of supervisors and
teachers.
3.   A detailed estimate of recurrent costs once the programs are developed
and impllmented on a national basis. Two costs should be provided, one with
and without an on-going staff for producing new programs.
Evaluation of the Prouosals
The MOEC will evaluate the proposals in collaboration with IDA.
Weighting will be given to the proposals according to the following
categories:



- 145 -
Pag 5 of 7
Technical proposal                              35 points
Consultant qualifications                       35 points
Experience of institutions in radio              15 points
Experience in supporting projects                15 points
in developing nations
Total                                            100 points
Once the proposals have been ranked in terms of overall quality, cost
proposals will be a factor in making the final choice.
Pusources to be Provided
1.   Technical assistance (12 staff years)
a.    Radio Producer (5 staff years)
This person will work with the Project Manager, radio producers,
and radio engineers providing overall direction for the project and producing
high quality programs. Specifically, this person will help to:
-     plan and lmpleuent all aspects of the project
-     build upon the Malawian experi'nce by incorporating as appropriate
the best experiences of instructional radio from other countries
-     design the instructional program in collaboration with the
curriculum developers
-     produce the programs with attontion to voice quality, sound
effects, music, pauses for student responses, etc.
-     provide training in all the above mentioned areas, but with
special emphasis on producing high quality programs
b.    Scriptwriter (4 staff years)
This person will be a specialist in writing effective scripts for
children.  Specifically, this person will work with counterpart scriptwriters
to:
-     design a general format for the scripts
-     develop alternative types of scripts that are appropriate for the
age of the children, that are effective, and that will give
variety to the lessons
-     ensure that the language used is appropriate for the children in
each standard
-    work with the curriculum specialists to ensure that the knowledge
and skills to be taught reflect a careful integration with the new
curriculum



- 146 -
ANNMU
Page 6 of 7
-     design entertainment segments to be interspersed In the programs
-     develop consistent and clear Instructions so the children
uAderstand what they are to do
c.    Evaluator (2.8 staff years)
This person will work with Malawian counterparts to carry out both
formative and summative evaluations. Specifically, this person will help tot
-     design and conduct a formative evaluation of the radio
lessons as they are being developed including the selection
of schools, instruments for classroom observations and
periodic achievement tests for the children
- _   design and conduct the summative evaluations including the
experimental design, selection of schools, development of
the testing instruments, administration of the tests, and
analyses of the data
d.    Alternative energy sources (0.1 staff years)
-     This person will carry out a study to determine what energy
source(s) to power the radios would be most cost effective
in Malawi.
e.    Management specialist for radio maintenance system (0.1 staff
years)
-     This person will conduct a study to determine what the most
cost-effective system would be for repairing the radios.
2.   Local Salaries (130 staff years)
IDA will provido the salaries of professional and support staff for the
project.
a.    Professional staff (82 staff years) -- Project Manager,
scriptwriters, radio producers, evaluators, radio operators, radio
engineer, materials specialist, teacher trainer and actors.
b.    Support staff (48 staff years) -- secretaries, accountant,
librarian, clerk, messengers, drivers, and watchmen.
3.    Other Direct Costs
These funds will cover the costs of producing materials, transportation,
communications, office rent, teacher training, radio transmission, and site
visits to other countries using instructional radio.



- 147 -
Page 7 of 7
4.    Equipment
Equipment will include 5,000 radios, power supplies (e.g. solar panels),
computers (5), office equipment and furniture, vehicles (3), and costs for
establishing the regional radio maintenance centers.
5.    Facilities
If needed, the Credit will cover construction costs for office space and
a studio.

Assumptions regarding capital costs (which are high) affect some of the
qualitative conclusions from rate of return analysis. Their inclusion affects
the upper levels, much more than primary, reflecting much higher building
costs at post-primary levels. The fall in rate of return estimates for Malawi
between 1981 and 1987 is caused by a combination of rising recurrent costs of
education and falling wage differentials especially between primary leavers
and the uneducated. Except for upper secondary (MCE), additional education
apparently no longer confers as substantial a wage-labor market advantage as
before.

ASSUMPTIONS FOR EDUCATION MINISTRY RECURRENT BUDGET PROJECTIONS
1. Projections are in real terms, in 1988 prices.
2.   Salaries and wages are assumed to remain at their 1988189 real
levels. Note that the projection of overall government recurrent
expenditure allows for a gradual recovery of civil service vages to their
1987/88 real level - real wage increases of 9 2 in 1989/90, 8 2 in 1990191
and 7 Z in 1991/92 are built in. If consistent assumptions about real wage
increases had been adopted, education's required share of the total would
have been larger. Additional projections will be done to quantify this
after further discussions during appraisal.
3.   Attrition of the teaching force is assumed to be 4 percent per year,
on the basis of a *best guess' by MOEC staff. This will receive more
attention (and possible amendation) during appraisal. The wage bill
increases by an average of 2.5Z each year due to wage drift - progression
up the salary scales with longer service.
4.   Capital formation (allowances for repairs and maintenance) is assumed
to remain constant in real terms. A small real increase should be built in
to allow for better maintenance. more repairs as facilities age, and
repairs to the. expanding stock of facilities.
5.   Administration:  The capital transfers component, the subventions to
MANEB and the MIE, are assumed to grow at 22 in real terms, reflecting the
added burdens on these bodies as primary enrollment grows. Personal
Emoluments (PE) grow 102 in real terms in 1989/90 reflecting increased
staffing, and remain constant in real terms thPreafter.
6.   Primary: Includes K2 per pupii earmarked bookfund (as agreed under
Credit 1767-MAl) from 1989/90. Other goods and services (G&S) provision
remains constant in real terms. However. this should be supplemented to
compensate for the loss of fee revenues to schools once school fees in the
lower standards are reduced.
7.   In the non-reform scenario, enrollments are assumed to grow at 3.92
per year, and the teaching force growth is determined by the output of the
PTTCs, given their present capacity (and new capacity which will come on-
stream during the period as existing projects are implemented). In the
reform scenario, enrollments grow at 7.9 2. The number of teachers
additional to the present output of the PTTCs, required to bring the
pupil:teacher ratio down to 55 is computed. It is assumed that in 1989/90
these will be recruited from trained teachers who are not presently
teaching, at a wage which is just above the teaching force average. The
proposed special in-service teacher training program is assumed to provide
the bulk of the additional teachers required in subsequent years. The
total costs of salaries are added into the budget projections, but not the
costs of training. The proposed credit will bear all training costs for
1989190 - 1993/94 and all salary costs until 1993/94, when the MOEC will
begin to share these costs.
8.   Secondary:  The reform scenario tliows anJollnent to grow by anr
additional amount compared to the new places being built under existing
projects, by assuming that an extra 4 pupils enroll per teacher after 1990.
Also, 6 urban schools are assumed to take a second shift of 400 pupils each



ANNEX TABLE 4
-154 -                       Fage 4 ot 4
from 1991192, and to receive a recurrent cost budget increase estimated at
balf the unit cost per pupil.
9. Primary Teacher Training: The reform scenario allows a 22 reduction
per year in unit costs by reallocating staff so as to increase
studenttstaff ratios, and adde 300 new places in 1994195 (provided under
the proposed credit).
10. Universitt Without reforms, the university budget grows at 6.72
annually in real terms. The reform scenario suts this by 32 from 1990/91.
11. Techincal/Vocational: The reform scenario assumes better capacity
utilization will allow a real unit cost reduction by 12 per year from
1990/91.
